Definition of Fiqh and Its Three Divisions
Defines fiqh with three necessary components and scriptural proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three components of fiqh according to the Hanafi usul definition and cite the Quranic and hadith evidences that support it.

Core Idea
Fiqh is not just knowledge of rulings but also deep understanding of texts and action upon them.

Fiqh is often understood simply as Islamic jurisprudence, but the classical Hanafi definition is more nuanced. This passage breaks fiqh into three essential parts: knowing the law, mastering its textual foundation and principles, and putting it into practice. The author supports this definition with Quranic verses and prophetic hadith, showing that complete fiqh requires both knowledge and action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions of fiqh
Read the first part of the passage and list the three components the author presents as necessary for complete fiqh. Note that the first is 'knowledge of what is legislated,' the second is 'mastery of knowledge' (understanding texts and connecting principles to branches), and the third is 'acting upon it.'
The second division is often overlooked—it involves deep comprehension, not mere memorization.
2
Locate the scriptural evidence for this definition
The author uses Quranic verses (2:269, 16:125, 9:122) and hadith to support the definition. Identify each evidence and see how it links to the three components. For example, verse 2:269 calls fiqh 'hikmah,' which combines knowledge and action.
The verse 'falawla nafara...' (9:122) is particularly strong—it uses the imperative 'liyatafaqqahu,' indicating obligation.
3
Analyze the linguistic argument for 'hikmah'
The author states that 'hikmah' in the Quran means both knowledge and action, citing Ibn Abbas. Consider how this linguistic point reinforces the third component (action). Without this, fiqh would be incomplete.
This shows that the definition is not arbitrary but rooted in scripture.
4
Understand the conclusiveness of the definition
The passage concludes that one who possesses all three is a 'complete faqih' (faqih mutlaq), while one lacking action is a faqih only in a limited sense. This hierarchy emphasizes the importance of practice.
This distinction affects how scholars evaluate each other and how students approach learning.
5
Apply the definition to a real scholarly context
Consider a student who memorizes all rulings but cannot derive new rulings from principles, and another who can derive but does not act. According to this definition, neither is a complete faqih. Why?
This step shows the practical implications of the definition for learning and teaching fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنوع الثاني : علم الفروع، وهو الفقه، وهو ثلاثة أقسام:
القسم الأول : علم المشروع بنفسه.
والقسم الثاني : إتقان المعرفة به، وهو معرفة النصوص بمعانيها، وضبط الأصول بفروعها.
والقسم الثالث : هو العمل به، حتى لا يصير نفس العلم مقصوداً.
فإذا تمت هذه الأوجه: كان فقهاً.
وقد دلَّ على هذا المعنى أن الله تعالى سمى علم الشريعة: حكمةً، فقال سبحانه: {يؤتي الحكمة من يشاء ومن يؤت الحكمة فقد أوتي خيراً كثيراً}.
وقد فسر ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما الحكمة في القرآن بعلم الحلال والحرام.
وقال تعالى: {ادع إلى سبيل ربك بالحكمة والموعظة الحسنة}. أي: بالفقه والشريعة.
والحكمة في اللغة: عبارةٌ عن العلم والعمل.
فمَن حوى هذه الجملة : كان فقيهاً مطلقاً، وإلا فهو فقيه من وجه دون وجه.
وقد نَدَبَ الله تعالى إليه بقوله سبحانه: {فلولا نفر من كل فرقة منهم طائفة ليتفقهوا في الدين ولينذروا قومهم إذا رجعوا إليهم}. وَصَفَهم بالإنذار، وهو الدعوة إلى العلم والعمل به.
وقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : «خياركم في الجاهلية خياركم في الإسلام إذا فقهوا».
وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «إذا أراد الله بعبد خيراً: يفقهه في الدين». اهـ
The second type: the science of branches, which is fiqh, and it has three divisions: The first division: knowledge of what is legislated in itself. The second division: mastery of knowledge of it, which is knowledge of the texts with their meanings, and connecting the principles to their branches. The third division: acting upon it, so that knowledge itself does not become the goal. When these aspects are complete, it is fiqh. This meaning is indicated by the fact that Allah called the science of Shariah 'hikmah', for He says: 'He gives wisdom to whom He wills, and whoever is given wisdom has been given much good.' Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) interpreted wisdom in the Quran as knowledge of the halal and haram. And He says: 'Call to the way of your Lord with wisdom and good admonition' meaning with fiqh and Shariah. Hikmah in language means knowledge and action. Whoever encompasses this totality is an absolute faqih; otherwise he is a faqih in one sense but not another. Allah encouraged it by His saying: 'Why should not a group from every section of them go forth to gain understanding in the religion and to warn their people when they return to them?' He described them with warning, which is calling to knowledge and acting upon it. And the Prophet said: 'The best of you in Jahiliyyah are the best of you in Islam when they gain understanding (faquhu).' And he said: 'When Allah wants good for a servant, He gives him understanding in the religion.'
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of fiqh (jurisprudence) as a composite of three essential components, each necessary for complete fiqh. The author first distinguishes the science of branches (fiqh) from the science of principles (usul). Fiqh is then divided into three parts: (1) knowledge of the law itself (what is prescribed), (2) deep understanding of the texts and the ability to connect principles to particular rulings, and (3) acting upon that knowledge. The purpose of learning is not mere theoretical knowledge but practice. The author then provides scriptural proofs: the Quranic verse about wisdom (hikmah) equated with fiqh by Ibn Abbas, and the verse about calling to the path of the Lord with wisdom, interpreted as fiqh and Shariah. Linguistically, hikmah means both knowledge and action. Thus, one who possesses all three components is a complete faqih; one lacking action is a faqih only in a limited sense. The verse about tafaqquh (gaining understanding) and the hadiths about the best people and divine intent for good reinforce the importance of understanding religion combined with warning and action.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): The second type of knowledge (after usul), consisting of knowledge of the law, deep understanding of texts and principles, and action upon it.
الحكمة (al-hikmah): In this context, synonymous with fiqh and Shariah, comprising both knowledge and action, as interpreted from Quranic usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the linguistic meaning of 'hikmah' that supports the definition of fiqh?
A) Knowledge of the unseen
B) Knowledge and action
C) Prophetic wisdom
D) Deep understanding of texts

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is a composite of knowledge, understanding, and action; a faqih is only complete when he acts upon what he knows.

Rukhsah in Necessity: Eating Haram Under Duress
Explaining the principle of concession (rukhsah) when necessity permits haram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that rukhsah permits haram under necessity while requiring compensation for rights of servants, and identify the qiyas basis for this rule.

Core Idea
Necessity permits the forbidden, but rights of others remain — liability is not waived.

This passage from a classical usul al-fiqh text examines how concession (rukhsah) interacts with both divine rights and individual rights when necessity forces a normally prohibited act. The author establishes that while the strict ruling ('azimah) yields to necessity, the rights of servants (huquq al-'ibad) are not eliminated; compensation is obligatory. An analogy with the muhrim (one in ihram) who may violate prohibitions under duress but must pay the penalty illustrates the principle. This lesson unpacks the rule, its conditions, and its proof structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine the central question: what is the hukm when necessity compels a person to consume something normally haram (e.g., carrion or another's property)? The rule must account for both divine prohibition and individual rights.
The issue is a conflict between two types of rights: rights of Allah (prohibition) and rights of servants (property).
2
Extract the Hanafi ruling
From the text, extract the ruling: consumption is permissible under necessity (rukhsah), but if the consumed item belongs to another, the consumer is liable (daman). The right of the servant is not waived.
The text states 'wa idha istawfa damina' — if he takes it, he is liable.
3
Analyze the dalil (qiyas)
The author provides an analogy: just as a muhrim under necessity may commit a prohibited act and then pays the penalty (jaza'), so too does the one in necessity eat haram but must compensate. Identify the 'illa (effective cause) for the ruling: the presence of necessity that does not eliminate the liability for rights of servants.
The analogy is qiyas jaliy: the muhrim's case is the asl, the hungry person's case is the far'.
4
Distinguish between rights
Note the distinction: rights of Allah (like prohibition) are more easily suspended in necessity, while rights of servants remain intact and require recompense. This is a key Hanafi usul principle.
The text contrasts huquq al-'ibad with rights of Allah.
5
Apply the rule to new scenarios
Consider similar cases: eating carrion of another's animal, or using someone else's medicine. The permittance depends on necessity, but compensation remains.
This rule extends to any violation of property rights under duress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من حقوق العباد و من جنس ما تحتمل السقوط من حقوق الله تعالى قسم أخذاً [؟] محتمل السقوط بصلح [؟] وليد السقوط [؟] المالم يوجد وهَا رَضَه [؟] فوقه وجب العَزْم بثبات الرخصة والعَزْم جب بصلح [؟] بنفعل أصل بازخلاص عزيمة وهذا لزاماً [؟] مخمصة خلال تناول طعام إلى إبقاء [؟] العريميات [؟] رخصه [؟] بمطلقه ماذا تركنا تكان شهيداً خلا فطعام نفسه واذا استوفى ضمنه لكون معصوماً في نفسه وذلك مثل تناول محظور الإحرام عن مرور [؟] بالمحرم إن يرخص له ويعمر الجزاء فكذلك ها هنا
From the rights of servants and from the kind that is likely to fall from the rights of Allah Almighty, a portion taken [?] likely to fall by reconciliation [?] ... and if not found, [?] above it, the resolve becomes obligatory by establishing concession, and the general [?] is binding by reconciliation [?] to do the original as a resolution, and this is necessary [?] hunger during eating food to preserve [؟] ... a concession [?] absolute? What we left as a witness except for his own food, and if he takes it, he is liable because it is inviolable in itself, and that is like eating a prohibited thing of ihram by passing [؟] for the muhrim: he is given concession and pays the penalty. So it is here.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage discusses a usul rule regarding the interaction between the rights of Allah and the rights of servants (huquq al-‘ibad) in the context of rukhsah (concession) due to necessity. The author states that when necessity arises, such as extreme hunger, what is normally haram (like carrion or another's property) becomes permissible to consume, but with the condition of liability (daman) if the property belongs to another person. This parallels the concession given to a muhrim (one in ihram) who, under necessity, may engage in a prohibited act but must pay the penalty (jaza'). The key principle is that 'azimah (resolve) yields to rukhsah under duress, but rights of servants (e.g., property rights) are not completely waived; the consumer must compensate. This is a standard Hanafi position on darurah.

Key Terms
الرخصة (al-rukhsah): A concession that permits an otherwise prohibited act under necessity or hardship.
العزيمة (al-'azimah): The original, strict ruling that applies in normal circumstances.
حقوق العباد (huquq al-'ibad): Rights belonging to individuals, such as property and personal inviolability.
الضمان (al-daman): Legal liability or compensation for harm or loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if someone in necessity eats another person's food?
A) It is completely permissible with no consequences.
B) It is permissible but he must compensate (daman) because it is a right of a servant.
C) It remains haram and he is sinful.
D) He must seek the owner's permission first.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, necessity permits the forbidden but does not eliminate compensation for rights of servants; liability remains.

Rukhsa: Concession, Not Absolute Permission – The Hanafi View on Liability
Explaining the ruling of concession in necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between azima and rukhsa and apply the rule that rukhsa does not eliminate the obligation of daman (compensation) for property consumed in necessity.

Core Idea
A concession (rukhsa) permits the act but does not remove liability for property consumed.

This passage from the Usul text defines rukhsa by contrasting it with absolute permission (ibaha mutlaqa). The author uses a concrete example—eating another's food in dire necessity—to show that even when necessity makes an act permissible, the other party's property rights are not completely extinguished. The analogy with a muhrim (one in ihram) who eats prohibited game under duress reinforces the point: a rukhsa is a limited dispensation, and residual obligations like compensation (daman or jaza') remain. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying Hanafi rulings in hardship cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms 'azima', 'rukhsa', and 'daman' in the passage. Note how the author defines each: azima is the original ruling, rukhsa is a concession, and daman is the guarantee that persists.
The text explicitly says 'la ibahata mutlaqa'—rukhsa is not absolute permission.
2
Parse the definition of rukhsa
Analyze the phrase 'rukhsa la ibahata mutlaqa'. What does 'mutlaqa' (absolute) imply? The author is rejecting the idea that necessity makes the act completely licit without any attached obligations.
This is a key usul point: rukhsa is conditional, not unconditional.
3
Understand the example
Consider the scenario of a person in dire necessity (muhsah) who eats another's food. According to the text, if he eats, he must guarantee (daman) the value; if he refrains and dies, he is a martyr. Why? Because the original ruling (prohibition) is still in effect, but a concession allows the act to preserve life—yet the property right remains.
4
Analyze the analogy (qiyas)
The author draws an analogy to a muhrim who, under necessity, eats prohibited game. The muhrim still owes the penalty (jaza'). This shows that a rukhsa does not nullify all consequences. Identify the asl (ihram case), far' (necessity case), and illah (preservation of underlying obligation).
The qiyas is explicit: 'wa dhalika mithl tanawul mahzur al-ihram... fadhalika hunna'.
5
Derive the principle
Synthesize: A rukhsa is a limited dispensation that permits a normally prohibited act due to necessity, but it does not remove residual obligations that protect the rights of others. In the example, daman is required because the owner's right is not overridden by the eater's need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السقوط با صلة لكن دليل السقوط المالم يوجد و عارض أمر وجب العمل به باثبات الرخصة والعمل وجب باصله باب جعل اصلا عزيمة و هذا من أصابته محصة حل لتناول طعام غيره رخصة لا إباحة مطلقة حتى اذا ترك نمات كان شهيدًا مخالف طعام نفسه واذا استوفان ضمنه لكونه مقدما في نفسه وذلك مثل تناول محظور الإحرام عن [؟] بالحيم أن [يرخص؟] لا ويضمن الجزاء فكذلك ههنا والله اعلم بالصواب
The dropping (of obligation) with a connection, but the proof of dropping does not exist, and something opposes that makes action necessary by establishing concession, and action is necessary by its origin (azima). This is from one who is struck by a crisis, it becomes permissible to eat the food of others as a concession, not an absolute permission, so that if he leaves (his own food?) and dies, he is a martyr, contrary to his own food. And if he consumes it, he guarantees it because he is prioritized in his own self. And that is like the consumption of the prohibited of ihram due to [?] to the sea? That it is a concession, and he does not guarantee the compensation? So similarly here. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a usul discussion clarifying the relationship between 'azima (original determination) and rukhsa (concession). The author states that a rukhsa is not an absolute license (ibaha mutlaqa) but a limited dispensation that preserves the underlying obligation. In the example of a person in dire need (muhsah), eating another's food is permitted as a rukhsa, but if he refuses to eat and dies, he is considered a martyr (shahid) for adhering to the original prohibition. If he does eat, he must guarantee (daman) the value because his own need does not override the owner's right entirely. This is analogous to a muhrim (one in ihram) who, when forced by necessity, may eat game (mahzur al-ihram) as a rukhsa but still owes the penalty (jaza'). Thus, a rukhsa does not nullify the original ruling's residual effects.

Key Terms
عزيمة (azima): The original, general ruling of a legal command without modification due to excuse.
رخصة (rukhsa): A concession that permits an otherwise prohibited act due to necessity, but not as an absolute permission.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for property consumed or damaged, here the value of the food eaten.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of a person who eats another's food in dire necessity?
A) The act is absolutely permissible (ibaha mutlaqa) with no obligation.
B) The act is a concession (rukhsa) but the eater must guarantee (daman) the value.
C) The act is forbidden because it takes another's property.
D) The eater is considered a martyr regardless of what he does.

Key Takeaway
Rukhsa is a limited dispensation that permits the act but does not remove liability for property consumed.

The Hanafi Definition of Fiqh
Defines fiqh as self-knowledge of rights and duties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of fiqh and distinguish it from a mere collection of rulings.

Core Idea
Fiqh is self-knowledge of what you owe and what you are owed before Allah.

This lesson examines the opening definition of Usul al-Bazdawi. The Hanafi school defines fiqh not as mere information about rules, but as a personal knowing (ma'rifa) of one's own obligations and rights. This definition shapes the entire Hanafi approach to legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the book
The passage begins by stating the book's subject: explaining usul al-fiqh (principles of jurisprudence). This sets the context for the definition that follows.
Usul al-fiqh is the methodology for deriving fiqh rulings.
2
Extract the key definition
The second sentence gives the Hanafi definition: 'الفقه عند الحنيفة معرفة النفس مالها وما عليها'. Break it down word by word.
The phrase is concise but packed with meaning.
3
Analyze the components: ma'rifa, al-nafs, ma laha, ma alayha
Ma'rifa (knowing) implies deeper experiential understanding, not just information. Al-nafs is the self. Ma laha (what it has) refers to rights, ma alayha (what it owes) refers to duties.
This makes fiqh a personal and accountable discipline.
4
Connect to the sources
The definition ends with 'اتّباعاً لكتاب الله وسنة رسوله' — following the Book of Allah and the Sunnah. This shows that the knowledge is derived from revelation.
The definition is not speculative but text-based.
5
Reflect on the practical consequence
Because fiqh is self-knowledge, it obligates the mukallaf to act upon it. It is not theoretical but immediately binding on the individual.
This distinguishes fiqh from other Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موضوع هذا الكتاب لبيان أصول الفقه. والفقه عند الحنيفة معرفة النفس مالها وما عليها. اتباعاً لكتاب الله وسنة رسوله.
The subject of this book is to explain the principles of jurisprudence. And fiqh according to the Hanafis is knowledge of the self, what it owes and what is owed to it. Following the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of His Messenger.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a definitional opening explaining the subject matter of the book and defining fiqh. The author states that the book's subject is to explain 'usul al-fiqh' (the principles of jurisprudence). He then defines 'fiqh' itself according to the Hanafi school: it is 'knowledge of the self, what it owes and what is owed to it' (معرفة النفس مالها وما عليها). This is the well-known definition attributed to Abu Hanifa. It means that fiqh is the knowledge by which a person understands his obligations (what he owes) and his rights (what is owed to him) in the sight of Allah. The definition grounds fiqh in self-knowledge and accountability, and the author connects it to following the Quran and Sunnah. The definition distinguishes fiqh from mere theoretical knowledge; it is practical and personal. The Hanafi definition emphasizes 'ma'rifa' (experiential knowledge) rather than mere 'ilm' (information).

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): Knowledge of the self's rights and duties (what it owes and is owed) based on detailed proofs.
أصول الفقه (usul al-fiqh): The principles and methodology used to derive fiqh rulings from their sources.
النفس (al-nafs): The self; the human being considered as an accountable individual.
مالها وما عليها (ma laha wa ma alayha): What it has (rights) and what it owes (duties).
اتباعاً (ittiba'an): Following; in accordance with.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, what is fiqh?
A) Knowledge of Allah's essence
B) Knowledge of the self, what it owes and what is owed to it
C) Knowledge of external legal rulings only
D) Knowledge of usul al-fiqh

Key Takeaway
Fiqh in the Hanafi school is personal knowledge of one's obligations and rights before Allah, rooted in Quran and Sunnah.

The Hanafi Definition of Fiqh: Three Essential Components
Defining fiqh and its three essential aspects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three divisions of fiqh according to al-Bazdawi and explain how the Quranic verse on hikmah supports the Hanafi definition.

Core Idea
Fiqh is not just knowing rulings—it must include understanding and acting upon that knowledge.

In this passage, al-Bazdawi presents a foundational definition of fiqh that goes beyond mere theoretical knowledge. He breaks fiqh into three inseparable divisions: knowing what is legislated, mastering the texts and principles, and acting upon that knowledge. A Quranic verse and the interpretation of Ibn ‘Abbas provide the proof that such comprehensive knowledge is a form of divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions of fiqh
Read the first part of the passage and list the three elements that together constitute fiqh according to al-Bazdawi.
The text explicitly numbers them: (1) knowledge of what is legislated itself, (2) mastery of knowledge (texts and principles), (3) acting upon it.
2
Understand the condition for fiqh
Note that fiqh is only complete when all three aspects are present. If any is missing, it is not true fiqh.
The author says: 'When these aspects are complete, it is fiqh.'
3
Examine the Quranic proof
Find the verse (2:269) and see how it links to fiqh through the term hikmah.
Allah calls knowledge of Shari‘ah 'wisdom', and Ibn Abbas interprets that as knowledge of halal and haram.
4
Connect the tafsir to the definition
Understand that Ibn Abbas's tafsir provides the direct link between hikmah and fiqh, making fiqh a form of divine wisdom.
This strengthens the argument that fiqh is not mere theory but a precious gift.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنوع الثاني : علم الفروع ، وهو الفقه، وهو ثلاثة أقسام: علم المشروع بنفسه. والقسم الثاني: إتقان المعرفة به، وهو معرفة النصوص بمعانيها، وضبط الأصول بفروعها. والقسم الثالث هو العمل به، حتى لا يصير نفس العلم مقصوداً. فإذا تمت هذه الأوجه : كان فقهاً.
وقد دلّ على هذا المعنى أن الله تعالى سمى علم الشريعة حكمةً، فقال سبحانه: ﴿يُؤْتِي الْحِكْمَةَ مَن يَشَاءُ وَمَن يُؤْتَ الْحِكْمَةَ فَقَدْ أُوتِيَ خَيْرًا كَثِيرًا﴾ [البقرة/269]. وقد فسر ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما الحكمة في القرآن بعلم الحلال والحرام.
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم، ربِّ يسر يا كريم، الحمد لله رب العالمين، اللهم صل على سيدنا محمد وآله وصحبه وسلم، وبعد: فيقول فقير رحمةِ ربِّه الغني الإمامُ العالمُ العلامة الشيخ أبو العدل قاسم بن قُطلُوبُغَا الحنفي، عامله الله بلطفه الخَفِي: هذا تخريج ما ذُكر في كتاب البزدوي في أصول الفقه الأحاديث والآثار، من علَّقته على وجه الإيجاز والاختصار، مُعَوِّلاً فيما لم يُصرح به الماتنُ على تصريح الشارح عبد العزيز البخاري رحمه الله. والله المستعان وعليه التكلان، ولا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله العلي العظيم. الخطبة : قوله : وقد فسَّر ابن عباس الحكمة في القرآن بعلم الحلال والحرام: ابن جرير الطبري ثنا المثنى ثنا عبد الله بن صالح ثنا معاوية عن علي عن ابن عباس في قوله: ﴿يُؤْتِي الْحِكْمَةَ مَن يَشَاءُ وَمَن يُؤْتَ الْحِكْمَةَ فقد أوتي خيرا كثيرا﴾ [البقرة/269] قال : يعني المعرفة بالقرآن ناسخه ومنسوخه، ومُحكمه ومتشابهه، -
And the second type: the science of branches, which is fiqh, and it has three divisions: the knowledge of what is legislated itself. And the second division: mastering knowledge of it, which is knowledge of the texts with their meanings, and tying the principles to their branches. And the third division is acting upon it, so that knowledge itself does not become the goal. When these aspects are complete, it is fiqh. And this meaning is indicated by the fact that Allah Almighty called the science of Shari‘ah wisdom (hikmah), for He said, exalted: 'He gives wisdom to whom He wills, and whoever is given wisdom has indeed been given abundant good' (Quran 2:269). And Ibn ‘Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, interpreted wisdom in the Quran as the knowledge of the lawful and the unlawful. [Footnote begins:] In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. O my Lord, make easy, O Generous. Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds. O Allah, send prayers upon our master Muhammad and his family and companions and grant peace. To proceed: the poor one in need of the mercy of his Lord, the Rich, the Imam, the scholar, the all-knowing, Shaykh Abu al-‘Adl Qasim ibn Qutlubugha al-Hanafi – may Allah treat him with His hidden kindness – says: This is a takhrij (verification of sources) for the hadiths and athar mentioned in the book al-Bazdawi in Usul al-Fiqh, from my commentary, done in brief and concise manner, relying, for what the author did not explicitly state, on the explicit statement of the commentator ‘Abd al-‘Aziz al-Bukhari, may Allah have mercy on him. Allah is the Helper and upon Him is reliance. There is no might and no power except with Allah, the Most High, the Almighty. [Section heading:] The Khutbah: His statement: And Ibn ‘Abbas interpreted wisdom in the Quran as the knowledge of the lawful and the unlawful: Ibn Jarir al-Tabari narrated to us: al-Muthanna narrated to us: ‘Abdullah ibn Salih narrated to us: Mu‘awiyah narrated from ‘Ali from Ibn ‘Abbas regarding His statement: 'He gives wisdom to whom He wills, and whoever is given wisdom has indeed been given abundant good' (Quran 2:269). He said: He means knowledge of the Quran: its abrogating and abrogated, its clear and its ambiguous, -
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a usul definition from al-Bazdawi's matn, defining the second type of knowledge (furu‘) as fiqh. Fiqh is not merely knowing rulings; it has three essential aspects: (1) knowing what is legally prescribed itself (the subject matter), (2) mastering understanding of the texts and their meanings, and connecting principles (usul) to their branches (furu‘), and (3) acting upon that knowledge to avoid making knowledge an end in itself. Only when all three are present is it true fiqh. The author supports this by citing the Quranic verse that calls the knowledge of Shari‘ah 'hikmah' (wisdom), and Ibn ‘Abbas interpreted hikmah as knowledge of halal and haram. The footnote added by Ibn Qutlubugha provides the chain of transmission for Ibn ‘Abbas's interpretation, emphasizing the importance of textual grounding. This definition distinguishes fiqh from mere theoretical knowledge.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): The knowledge of what is legislated itself, mastery of knowledge of it (texts and principles), and acting upon it.
الحكمة (al-hikmah): The knowledge of the lawful and unlawful, i.e., fiqh, as interpreted by Ibn Abbas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazdawi, what are the three divisions of fiqh?
A) Knowledge of rulings, mastery of texts, and acting upon them.
B) Fard, wajib, and sunnah.
C) Usul, furu‘, and adab.
D) Ibadat, mu‘amalat, and ‘uqubat.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh in the Hanafi school is a comprehensive concept that integrates knowledge of rulings, deep understanding of textual evidence, and practical application—all three are essential for it to be considered true fiqh.

Definition of Fiqh: Knowledge with Action
Definition of fiqh with textual proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fiqh and identify the key condition that distinguishes it from mere knowledge.

Core Idea
Fiqh is knowledge of Sharia rulings coupled with action; without practice, it is incomplete.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul defines fiqh not merely as theoretical understanding but as applied knowledge. The author uses Quranic verses, a poetic example, and hadith to establish that a true faqih is one who acts upon his knowledge. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the Hanafi approach to jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
The passage defines fiqh as 'knowledge with the quality of proficiency, connected with action.' This is the central claim: fiqh is not just knowing rulings but also practicing them.
2
Examine the Quranic proof from al-Nahl 16:125
The verse uses 'ḥikmah' which the author interprets as fiqh and Sharia. Since ḥikmah linguistically means knowledge and action, the verse supports the definition of fiqh as knowledge with action.
The verse is cited as evidence that fiqh must be accompanied by practice.
3
Analyze the poetic evidence
The poet calls a physician 'faqih' because he knows what is beneficial and harmful and acts upon that knowledge. This illustrates that the term 'faqih' implies practical application.
The example shows the linguistic usage of 'faqih' in Arabic.
4
Evaluate the Quranic call to tafaquh in al-Tawbah 9:122
Allah commands a group to gain understanding (tafaqquh) in religion and then warn their people. Warning (inzār) is calling to knowledge and action, reinforcing that fiqh leads to practice.
The verse links understanding with the duty to teach and act.
5
Consider the prophetic hadith evidence
The Prophet ﷺ said that when Allah desires good for a servant, He grants him understanding of the religion, and that the best in Jahiliyyah are the best in Islam when they acquire fiqh. These hadiths emphasize the virtue of fiqh and its association with divine favor.
The hadiths confirm that fiqh is a praiseworthy quality that should motivate action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحلال والحرام.
وقال تعالى: ادْعُ إِلَى سَبِيلِ رَبِّكَ بِالْحِكْمَةِ وَالْمَوْعِظَةِ الْحَسَنَةِ النحل / ١٢٥ . أي: بالفقه والشريعة.
والحكمة في اللغة: عبارةٌ عن العلم والعمل.
وكذلك موضع اشتقاق هذا الاسم، وهو الفقه : دليل عليه، وهو العلم بصفة الإتقان، مع اتصال العمل به.
قال الشاعر :
أرسلتُ فيها قَرْماً ذا إقحام طباً فقيهاً بذوات الأبلام) سماه : فقيهاً ؛ لعلمه بما يَصلُح ، وبما لا يصلح، والعمل به. فمَن حوى هذه الجملة : كان فقيهاً مطلقاً وإلا فهو فقيه من وجه، دون وجه.
وقد نَدَبَ الله تعالى إليه بقوله سبحانه: ﴿فَلَوْلَا نَفَرَ مِن كُلِ فِرْقَةٍ مِّنْهُمْ طَائِفَةٌ لِيَتَفَقَّهُوا فِي الدِّينِ وَلِيُنذِرُوا قَوْمَهُمْ إِذَا رَجَعُوا إِلَيْهِمْ﴾ التوبة / ١٢٢. وَصَفَهم بالإنذار، وهو الدعوة إلى العلم والعمل به.
وقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: خياركم في الجاهلية خياركم في الإسلام إذا فقهوا و مقدمه ومؤخَرِه ، وحلاله وحرامه، وأخرجه ابن أبي حاتم عن أبيه عن أبي صالح به سنداً ومتناً، وأخرجا عن مجاهد قال : «هو العلم والفقه والقرآن».
وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «إذا أراد الله بعبد خيراً : يُفقهه الدين».
وأصحابنا رحمهم الله هم السابقون في هذا الباب، ولهم الرتبةُ العليا، والدرجة القصوى في علم الشريعة وهم الربانيون في علم الكتاب والسنة، وملازمة القدوة.
وهم أصحاب الحديث والمعاني.
The lawful and the unlawful. And He said, Most High: 'Invite to the way of your Lord with wisdom and good instruction' (al-Nahl 16:125), meaning: with fiqh and Sharia. And ḥikmah in language is an expression for knowledge and action. And likewise the derivation of this name, which is fiqh, is evidence for it, and it is knowledge with the quality of proficiency, connected with action. The poet said: 'I sent among them a noble man of knowledge, a physician, a faqih in matters of colic' – he called him faqih because of his knowledge of what is beneficial and what is harmful, and acting upon it. Whoever possesses this totality is a faqih absolutely; otherwise he is a faqih in one aspect, not another. And Allah, Most High, summoned to it by His saying: 'If only a group from every section of them would go forth to gain understanding in the religion and warn their people when they return to them' (al-Tawbah 9:122). He described them as warning, which is the call to knowledge and action upon it. And the Prophet ﷺ said: 'The best of you in Jahiliyyah are the best of you in Islam when they gain understanding' – its front and back, its lawful and unlawful. And Ibn Abi Hatim narrated it from his father from Abu Salih with chain and text, and they (Bukhari and Muslim) narrated from Mujahid who said: 'It is knowledge, understanding, and the Quran.' And he ﷺ said: 'When Allah desires good for a servant, He grants him understanding of the religion.' And our companions, may Allah have mercy on them, are the foremost in this chapter; they have the highest rank and the utmost degree in the knowledge of Sharia; they are the rabbanites in knowledge of the Book and Sunnah, and adherence to the example. And they are the people of Hadith and meanings.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a definition of fiqh from the Hanafi usul tradition. It begins by connecting fiqh to ḥikmah (wisdom) as used in the Quran, explaining that ḥikmah linguistically means knowledge combined with action. The author then turns to the etymology of the word 'fiqh' itself, showing that it implies knowledge with precision and practice. A poetic evidence is cited to illustrate that calling someone a faqih requires both knowledge of what is suitable and acting accordingly. Hence, true fiqh is comprehensive: mere theoretical knowledge without practice yields only a partial fiqh. Quranic support is brought from verse 9:122, where 'tafaqquh' (gaining understanding) is linked to 'inzar' (warning), which is calling to knowledge and action. Two hadiths are then quoted: one stating that the best in Jahiliyyah are the best in Islam when they acquire fiqh, and another that when Allah wants good for a person, He gives them fiqh in religion. Finally, the author praises the Hanafi scholars (Ashābunā, i.e., the followers of Abu Hanifa) as the foremost in this science, describing them as 'rabbānīyūn' (divinely learned) and 'asḥāb al-ḥadīth wa al-ma‘ānī' (people of hadith and meanings), indicating their mastery of both transmitted texts and rational understanding. This passage establishes that fiqh is not mere information but applied knowledge, and that the Hanafi school embodies this ideal.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): Knowledge of the rulings of Sharia with the quality of proficiency, connected with action.
الحكمة (al-ḥikmah): In this context, it means knowledge combined with action, synonymous with fiqh and Sharia.
الإنذار (al-inzār): Warning or calling others to knowledge and action upon it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Memorizing many hadith
B) Knowledge of rulings with action
C) Being from the Hanafi school
D) Ability to derive rulings independently

Key Takeaway
Fiqh in the Hanafi tradition is applied knowledge: one is not a true faqih unless knowledge is accompanied by action.

The Imperative Form is Literal and Binding
Establishing that the imperative form is literal and binding for obligation using linguistic analogy and prophetic practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the imperative form (sīghat al-amr) is literal (haqiqah) and binding (lazimah) by identifying the operative evidence and distinguishing it from metaphorical usage.

Core Idea
The imperative form is literal and binding for obligation; only a clear evidence can divert it.

Understanding the imperative form's default ruling is foundational to Hanafi usul. This passage from al-Bazdawi argues from linguistic analogy and prophetic practice that when Allah or the Prophet commands using the imperative form, the default is obligation (wujūb). The student must grasp this principle to derive rulings from commands in the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic analogy
The author parallels other verbal forms (past, present, future) that have dedicated expressions; command must similarly have a dedicated expression that obligates action.
2
Define haqiqah vs. majaz
Haqiqah cannot be negated from its referent (e.g., 'father' for closest father), while majaz can be negated (e.g., 'father' for grandfather).
3
Apply to the imperative form
One can say 'So-and-so did not command today' despite many actions, but negating the imperative expression itself is invalid, proving it is haqiqah and binding.
4
Analyze the hadith evidence
The Prophet disapproved of companions imitating his actions (taking off sandals, continuous fasting), showing that mere action is not command; only explicit imperative is binding.
5
Extract the principle
The imperative form is literal and binding; it indicates obligation (wujub) unless a qarina diverts it to another meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد وجدنا كل مقاصد الفعل، مثلُ الماضي والحال والاستقبال مختصةً بعبارات وضعت لها، فالمقصود بالأمر كذلك أن يكون مختصاً يجب بالعبارة، وهذا المقصود من أعظم المقاصد، فهو بذلك أولى.
وإذا ثبت أصل الموضوع كان حقيقةً، فتكون لازمةً إلا بدليل ألا يُرى أن أسماء الحقائق لا تسقط عن مسمياتها أبداً، وأما المجاز فيصح نفيه.
يقال للأب الأقرب أب، لا ينتفي عنه بحال، ويسمى الجد : أباً، ويصح نفيه.
ثم ههنا صح أن يقال : إن فلاناً لم يأمر اليوم بشيء مع كثرة أفعاله، وإذا تكلم بعبارة الأمر : لم يستقم نفيه.
وقد قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حين خلع نعليه، فخلع الناس نعالهم منكراً عليهم : ما لكم خلعتُم نعالكم ؟ ).
وأنكر عليهم الموافقة في وصال الصوم، فقال: «إني أبيتُ يُطعمني ربي ويسقيني».
فثبت أن صيغة الأمر لازمة.
وس
ولا ننكر تسميته مجازاً؛ لأن الفعل يجب به، فسُمِّي به مجازاً، والنبي عليه الصلاة والسلام دعا إلى الموافقة بلفظ الأمر بقوله: «صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي، فدل أن صيغة الأمر لازمةٌ.
من ذلك : باب موجب الأمر :
(حديث عن ابن عمر أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن الوصال، قالوا: إنك تواصل ؟ قال : إني لستُ كهيئتكم، إني أُطعم وأُسقى». وفي رواية: «لستُ مثلكم». متفق عليه وللبخاري: «أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم واصل : فواصل الناس فشق عليهم، فنهاهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يواصلوا، قالوا: إنك تواصل ؟ قال : لستُ كهيئتكم، إني أظلُّ أُطعم وأسقى».
وأخرج الموطأ وأبو داود الرواية الأولى.
وعن أنس قال: «واصل رسولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في آخر شهر رمضان، فواصل ناس من المسلمين، فبلغه ذلك ، فقال : لو مُدَّ لنا الشهر لواصلنا وصالاً يدع المتعمقون تعمقهم، إنكم لستم مثلي، أو قال: لستُ مثلكم، إني أظل يطعمني ربي ويسقيني».
وفي رواية : قال : قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «لا تواصلوا. قالوا: إنك تواصل؟ قال: لستُ كأحد منكم إني أبيت أطعم وأسقى». أخرجه البخاري ومسلم.
وأخرج الترمذي الثانية، وقال: «إن ربي يطعمني ويسقيني».
وأخرجاه من حديث أبي هريرة، وفيه أني أبيت يطعمني ربي ويسقيني.
And we find that all purposes of the verb, such as past, present, and future, are specific to expressions put for them. Thus, the purpose of command is likewise that it be specific and obligatory by expression. This purpose is among the greatest purposes, so it is more entitled to this. And if the origin of the subject is established, it is literal, so it is binding unless there is evidence. Do you not see that names of realities never cease from their referents ever? As for metaphor, its negation is valid. It is said of the closest father 'father'; it is never negated from him, and the grandfather is called 'father', and its negation is valid. Then here it is valid to say: 'So-and-so did not command anything today' despite his many actions, but if he speaks with the expression of command, its negation is not sound. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said when he took off his sandals, and the people took off their sandals, disapproving of them: 'Why did you take off your sandals?' And he disapproved of their agreement in continuous fasting, saying: 'I spend the night with my Lord feeding me and giving me drink.' Thus it is established that the imperative form is binding. And we do not deny calling it metaphor because the action is obligated by it, so it is called metaphor. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, called to conformity with the word of command by his saying: 'Pray as you see me praying.' So it indicates that the imperative form is binding. From that: Chapter on the ruling of the imperative: (Hadith from Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet forbade continuous fasting. They said: 'You practice continuous fasting?' He said: 'I am not like you; I am fed and given drink.') In another narration: 'I am not like you.' Agreed upon. And in Bukhari: 'The Prophet practiced continuous fasting, so the people practiced continuous fasting and it became difficult for them, so the Messenger of Allah forbade them from continuous fasting. They said: 'You practice continuous fasting?' He said: 'I am not like you; I remain fed and given drink.'' And Malik and Abu Dawud narrated the first narration. And from Anas: 'The Messenger of Allah practiced continuous fasting at the end of Ramadan, so some Muslims practiced continuous fasting. That reached him, so he said: 'If the month were extended for us, we would have practiced continuous fasting such that the exaggerators would leave their exaggeration. You are not like me' - or he said: 'I am not like you; I remain with my Lord feeding me and giving me drink.' In another narration: He said: The Prophet said: 'Do not practice continuous fasting.' They said: 'You practice continuous fasting?' He said: 'I am not like any of you; I spend the night being fed and given drink.' Narrated by Bukhari and Muslim. And Tirmidhi narrated the second, saying: 'My Lord feeds me and gives me drink.' And both narrated from Abu Hurairah, with the wording: 'I spend the night with my Lord feeding me and giving me drink.'
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a legal reasoning (istidlal) from the matn of al-Bazdawi that establishes the imperative form (sigha al-amr) as a literal (haqiqah) and binding (lazimah) expression for command. The author argues by analogy with other verbal forms: just as past, present, and future have dedicated expressions, so command must have a dedicated expression that obligates action. Once established as a linguistic reality (haqiqah), it is binding unless evidence indicates otherwise (qarīnah). The author contrasts haqiqah with majaz: haqiqah cannot be negated from its referent, while majaz can. For example, the closest father is always called 'father' (haqiqah), but a grandfather can be called 'father' metaphorically and that metaphor can be negated. Applying this to the imperative: one can say 'So-and-so did not command today' even if he performed many actions, but if he used the imperative expression, its negation is invalid. The author supports this with two hadith. First, when the Prophet removed his sandals during prayer and the companions followed, he disapproved, indicating that mere action (fi‘l) does not constitute a command. Second, the Prophet disapproved of companions imitating his continuous fasting (wisāl), because his action was specific to him due to divine sustenance. Thus, action alone does not indicate command; only the imperative form does. The author then addresses a counterpoint: we do not deny that the imperative can be called 'metaphor' because it necessitates action. But the Prophet himself used the imperative in 'Pray as you see me praying' (صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي), which shows that the imperative form is binding and implies obligation (wujūb). The passage concludes with the heading 'Chapter on the ruling of the imperative' (باب موجب الأمر) and provides the hadith on continuous fasting as further evidence that the imperative form is literal and binding, as the Prophet commanded 'Do not practice continuous fasting' and his own exception was due to his special status.

Key Terms
صيغة الأمر (ṣīghat al-amr): The linguistic form used to express a command, typically the imperative verb form (if‘al).
حقيقة (ḥaqīqah): A word used in its original, conventional meaning; its application cannot be negated.
مجاز (majāz): A word used in a non-original meaning due to a relationship; its application can be negated.
موجب الأمر (mūjib al-amr): The ruling that results from the imperative form, i.e., obligation (wujūb).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the status of the imperative form (ṣīghat al-amr)?
A) It is always metaphorical (majaz) unless proven literal.
B) It is literal (haqiqah) and binding (lazimah) unless there is evidence to the contrary.
C) It indicates a request (talab) but not obligation.
D) It can be negated freely like an action.

Key Takeaway
The imperative form in the Quran and Sunnah is literal and binding, indicating obligation unless there is clear evidence to the contrary.

The Command Implies Obligation
Arguing that the imperative form denotes obligation with proofs and refutations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi usul principle that the imperative form (sigha al-amr) indicates obligation (wujub), and apply it to derive rulings from commands in the Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
The command demands obligation by default; permission or recommendation requires a separate indicator.

In Hanafi usul, the imperative form (sigha al-amr) is not neutral—it carries a heavy presumption of obligation. This passage from al-Bazdawi establishes that presumption through Quranic verses, consensus, and rational argument. Understanding this principle is critical for deriving fiqh rulings from divine commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
State the central thesis: the command (amr) is for obligation (wujub), not permission or recommendation.
This is the default Hanafi position.
2
Examine the Quranic Proofs
Analyze verses 30:25 and 24:63. The first attributes the standing of heaven and earth to the command, showing existence is intended. The second warns against opposing the command, implying obligation.
These are dalil naqli (textual proofs).
3
Understand the Rational Proof
Consider the transitive nature of the command: its necessary concomitant (lazim) is compliance, just as breaking implies being broken. Therefore the command inherently demands obligation.
This is a qiyas jali argument based on linguistic structure.
4
Refute the Waqifiyya
The 'waqifiyya' suspend judgment on the command's meaning. The passage argues this would invalidate all linguistic realities, which is impossible.
Waqifiyya is rejected because it undermines certainty in revelation.
5
Evaluate the Dispute on Permission/Recommendation
Some scholars claim the command is literal for ibaha and nadb. The Hanafi response treats those uses as metaphorical (majaz), not literal. The author notes a stronger view that permission and recommendation are included in the scope of obligation (in estimation).
The preferred view (asahh) is that obligation encompasses both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تعطيل. وقد أجرى سنته في الإيجاد بعبارة الأمر ، فلو لم يكن الوجود مقصوداً بالأمر : لما استقام قرينة للإيجاد بعبارة الأمر. وقال الله تعالى: ﴿وَمِنْ ءَايَنِهِ أَن تَقُومَ السَّمَاءُ وَالْأَرْضُ بِأَمْرِهِ الروم/ ٢٥ . فقد نَسَبَ وأضاف القيام إلى الأمر، وذلك دليل على حقية الوجود مقصوداً بالأمر. وقال الله تعالى: ﴿ فَلْيَحْذَرِ الَّذِينَ يُخَالِفُونَ عَنْ أَمْروه ﴾ النور / ٦٣ . وكذلك دلالة الإجماع حجةٌ ؛ لأن من أراد طلب فعل: لم يكن في وسعه أن يطلبه إلا بلفظ الأمر. وأما الدليل المعقول أن تصاريف الأفعال وضعت لمعان على الخصوص، كسائر العبارات، فصار معنى المضي للماضي حقاً لازماً، إلا بدليل. وكذلك الحال واحتمال أن يكون من الاستقبال: لا يُخرجه عن موضوعه، فكذلك صيغة الأمر لطلب المأمور به فيكون حقاً لازماً به على أصل الوضع. ألا يُرى أن الأمر فعل متعد، لازمه انتمر، ولا وجود للمتعدي إلا أن يثبت لازمه ، كالكسر لا يتحقق إلا بالانكسار. فقضية الأمر لغةً: أن لا يثبت إلا بالامتثال، إلا أن ذلك لو ثبت بالأمر نفسه : لسقط الاختيار من المأمور أصلاً. وللمأمور عندنا ضرب من الاختيار وإن كان ضرورياً، فنَقْلُ حكم الوجود إلى الوجوب حقاً لازماً بالأمر لا يتوقف على اختيار المأمور، وتَوَقَّفَ الوجودُ على اختيار المأمور؛ صيانةً واحترازاً عن الجبر، فلذلك صار الأمر للإيجاب، والله أعلم. ولو وجب التوقف في حكم الأمر لوجب في النهي، فيصير حكمهما واحداً، وهو باطل. وما اعتبره الواقفيةُ من الاحتمال يُبطل الحقائق كلها، وذلك مُحالٌ، : ألا يُرى أَنَّا لم ندَّعِ أنه مُحكَمٌ. وإذا أريد بالأمر الإباحةُ أو الندب: فقد زعم بعضُهم أنه حقيقةٌ. وقال الكرخي والجصاص : بل هو مجاز؛ لأن اسم الحقيقة لا يتردد بين النفي والإثبات، فلما جاز أن يقال: إني غير مأمور بالنقل: دل أنه مجاز؛ لأنه جاز أصله وتعداه. ووجه القول الآخر: أن معنى الإباحة والندب من الوجوب: بعضه في التقدير، كأنه قاصر ، لا مغاير؛ لأن الوجوب ينتظمه، وهذا أصح.
And no suspension. He has applied His custom in bringing into existence by the expression of command. So if existence were not intended by the command, it would not be a valid indicator for bringing into existence by the expression of command. Allah said: 'And among His signs is that the heaven and earth stand by His command' (30:25). He attributed and connected standing to the command, and that is proof that existence is truly intended by the command. Allah said: 'So let those beware who oppose His command' (24:63). Likewise, the indication of consensus is a proof, because one who wants to request an action cannot request it except with the wording of command. As for the rational proof: the forms of verbs are placed for specific meanings like other expressions, so the past tense meaning is a necessary right for the past, unless there is evidence. Similarly, the possibility of future does not remove it from its subject meaning. So also the imperative form is for requesting the commanded act, so it becomes a necessary right by it according to the original placement. Do you not see that the command is a transitive verb, its necessary concomitant is compliance, and the transitive has no existence unless its necessary concomitant is established, like breaking does not occur except by being broken. The implication of command linguistically is that it is not established except by compliance, but if that were established by the command itself, the choice of the commanded person would be nullified entirely. In our view, the commanded has a kind of choice, even if compelled. So transferring the ruling of existence to obligation as a necessary right by the command does not depend on the choice of the commanded; existence depends on the choice of the commanded to safeguard against coercion. Therefore the command is for obligation, and Allah knows best. If hesitation in the ruling of the command were necessary, it would be necessary in prohibition, making their ruling one, which is false. What the fatalists consider of possibility invalidates all realities, and that is impossible. Do you not see that we do not claim it is decisive? When command is intended for permission or recommendation, some claim it is literal. Al-Karkhi and al-Jassas said it is metaphorical, because the name of literal truth is not ambiguous between negation and affirmation; when it is possible to say 'I am not commanded to transfer', it indicates it is metaphorical because its origin and exceeding are valid. The other view: the meaning of permission and recommendation from obligation is part of it in estimation, as if it is limited, not different, because obligation encompasses it. This is more correct.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the nature of the command (amr) in usul al-fiqh. The author argues that the imperative form (sigha al-amr) is placed for obligation (wujub), not for permission or recommendation. The text begins by stating the principle without suspension (ta'til). It then provides three proofs: first, a Quranic proof from verse 30:25 where Allah attributes the standing of the heavens and earth to His command, showing that existence is intended by the command; second, verse 24:63 warns against opposing the command, implying obligation; third, consensus (ijma) that no one can request an action except by the wording of command. A rational proof follows: verb forms have specific meanings, and the imperative is for requesting the act, so it necessarily implies obligation. The author then addresses a potential objection: if the command itself required compliance, the commanded would have no choice; but Hanafis hold that the commanded has a degree of choice (though compelled), so the command transfers the ruling of existence to obligation, while actual existence depends on choice, avoiding jabr (coercion). The passage also refutes the 'waqifiyya' (those who suspend judgment) who argue that the command could be for something else, saying such possibility would invalidate all realities. Finally, it discusses whether using the command for permission (ibaha) or recommendation (nadb) is literal or metaphorical. Some claim it is literal, but al-Karkhi and al-Jassas say it is metaphorical because the term 'literal' would not allow both affirmation and negation; the other view holds that permission and recommendation are part of obligation in estimation. The author prefers the view that the command is for obligation.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The command, specifically the imperative form (sigha) used to request an action from a superior to an inferior.
الوجوب (al-wujub): Legal obligation; the ruling that an act is required without latitude.
الواقفية (al-waqifiyya): A group who suspend judgment on whether the command implies obligation or something else, claiming it is open to multiple possibilities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul position presented in this passage, what does the imperative form (sigha al-amr) indicate by default?
A) Permission (ibaha)
B) Recommendation (nadb)
C) Obligation (wujub)
D) Suspension (waqf)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that the imperative form indicates obligation by default is foundational for deriving fiqh rulings from divine commands.

Command After Prohibition: Hanafi View of Obligation
Determining that a command after a prohibition indicates obligation, not permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a command after prohibition indicates obligation and evaluate the opposing argument based on the verse of hunting.

Core Idea
A command after prohibition does not become permission; it remains obligatory by default.

This lesson tackles a key usul al-fiqh principle: whether a command that follows a prohibition changes its default meaning. The Hanafi school holds firm that the imperative form retains its original force of obligation. We will examine the proof and the counter-argument from the Quranic verse about hunting after ihram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and the dispute
What is the Hanafi position on a command after prohibition? Who disagrees and on what basis?
The rule is that it indicates obligation (wujub) unless there is specific evidence to the contrary.
2
Analyze the Hanafi dalil
The author says 'istidlalan bi aslihi wa sighatihi' (based on its origin and form). Understand that in Hanafi usul, the imperative form (sigha) originally signifies obligation, and prohibition does not alter this default.
No specific textual evidence is cited; the argument is based on the nature of command.
3
Examine the opposing dalil
Some scholars cite Quran 5:2: 'When you have completed the pilgrimage, then hunt' (fa'stadu). They argue that since hunting was prohibited during ihram, the command after the prohibition indicates permission or recommendation.
The verse is the key evidence for the opposing view.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that the verse does not provide a qarina (indication) strong enough to shift the default meaning of the imperative from obligation to permission. The command remains obligatory unless additional evidence (e.g., a context of permission) is present.
The default rule of 'al-amr yufid al-wujub' is not overridden by mere removal of prohibition.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a person is commanded to do an act after a prohibition, a Hanafi considers it obligatory to perform, while others consider it merely permissible or recommended. This affects whether omission is sinful or not.
For example, hunting after leaving ihram: Hanafi says it is wajib (obligatory) as an act of worship? Actually, the verse is about permissibility after prohibition, but the principle applies generally. The thamarah is in similar cases where a command follows a prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتصل بهذا الأصل: أن الأمر بعد الحظر لا يتعلق بالندب ،والإباحة لا محالة بل هو للإيجاب عندنا، إلا بدليل؛ استدلالاً بأصله وصيغته. ومنهم من قال بالندب والإباحة ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا حَلَلْتُمْ فَأَصْطَادُوا ﴾ المائدة / ٢ .
And connected to this principle: The command after prohibition does not pertain to recommendation or permission at all, but rather it is for obligation in our view, unless there is evidence; based on its origin and form. Some say it is for recommendation and permission, due to His saying: 'And when you have completed the pilgrimage, then hunt' (5:2).
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the principle of 'command after prohibition' (amr ba'd al-hazr). The author states that when a command is given after a previous prohibition, it does not indicate recommendation (nadb) or permission (ibaha) directly; rather, it indicates obligation (wujub) according to the Hanafi school, unless there is evidence (dalil) to the contrary. The reasoning is based on the original meaning of the command form (sigha) which is for obligation. The passage then mentions that some scholars (likely from other schools) hold that such a command indicates recommendation or permission, citing the Quranic verse (5:2) where after the prohibition of hunting during ihram, Allah says 'when you have completed the pilgrimage, then hunt' – they argue this shows permission. However, the Hanafi position is that the default of the command is obligation, and the verse does not override that default without additional evidence.

Key Terms
الأمر بعد الحظر (al-amr ba'd al-hazr): A command that comes after a prohibition, e.g., 'hunt' after being prohibited from hunting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permission (ibaha)
B) Recommendation (nadb)
C) Obligation (wujub)
D) No ruling

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a command after prohibition retains its original implication of obligation unless a specific indicator overrides it.

The Nature of Command: Haqiqah for Obligation
Defining amr and defending its haqiqah for wujub

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the haqiqah of al-amr and apply it to determine the ruling of a command without contextual evidence.

Core Idea
A command (amr) in its true linguistic reality indicates obligation; any other use is metaphorical.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the word 'amr' (command) is a foundational term. This lesson examines whether it is haqiqah (literal) for obligation alone or also for permission and recommendation. The Hanafi school, represented by al-Karkhi and al-Jassas, maintains that amr is haqiqah only for wujub, and other uses are majaz (metaphorical). This principle has a direct impact on how legal rulings are derived from commands in the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Define amr, haqiqah, majaz, wujub, ibaha, nadb, and wāqifiyya. Understand their linguistic and technical meanings in Hanafi usul.
The term 'haqiqah' refers to the original, literal meaning for which a word was coined.
2
Analyze the refutation of the Wāqifiyya
The Wāqifiyya suspended judgment on the meaning of amr, arguing it only indicates probability. The text refutes this by showing that such suspension would destroy all legal realities (haqa'iq).
Hanafi usul affirms certainty (qat') in the meaning of amr for wujub, not probability.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof for haqiqah of wujub
The proof is that one can truthfully say 'I am not commanded' when performing a non-obligatory act (e.g., voluntary acts). This negation is only possible if amr is haqiqah for wujub; otherwise, the negation would be false.
This is a rational (aqli) proof based on linguistic usage.
4
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: when a command appears without a contextual indicator (qarina), the Hanafi rule is that it indicates obligation. Other schools may require evidence to determine the degree.
This difference affects how one interprets commands in primary sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمأمور عندنا ضرب من الاختيار وإن كان ضرورياً، فنَقْلُ حكم الوجود إلى الوجوب حقاً لازماً بالأمر لا يتوقف على اختيار المأمور، وتَوَقَّفَ الوجودُ على اختيار المأمور؛ صيانةً واحترازاً عن الجبر، فلذلك صار الأمر للإيجاب، والله أعلم.
ولو وجب التوقف في حكم الأمر لوجب في النهي، فيصير حكمهما واحداً، وهو باطل.
وما اعتبره الواقفيةُ من الاحتمال يُبطل الحقائق كلها، وذلك مُحالٌ، : ألا يُرى أَنَّا لم ندَّعِ أنه مُحكَمٌ.
وإذا أريد بالأمر الإباحةُ أو الندب: فقد زعم بعضُهم أنه حقيقةٌ. وقال الكرخي والجصاص : بل هو مجاز؛ لأن اسم الحقيقة لا يتردد بين النفي والإثبات، فلما جاز أن يقال: إني غير مأمور بالنقل: دل أنه مجاز؛ لأنه جاز أصله وتعداه.
ووجه القول الآخر: أن معنى الإباحة والندب من الوجوب: بعضه في التقدير، كأنه قاصر ، لا مغاير؛ لأن الوجوب ينتظمه، وهذا أصح.
For the commanded person according to us there is a kind of choice even if it is necessary. So transferring the ruling of existence to obligation as a true binding thing by the command does not depend on the choice of the commanded person; existence depended on the choice of the commanded person as a preservation and avoidance of compulsion. Therefore the command is for obligation, and Allah knows best. If suspension were necessary in the ruling of command, it would be necessary in prohibition, and then the ruling of both would be the same, which is false. What the Wāqifiyya consider of probability invalidates all realities, and that is impossible. Do you not see that we do not claim it is muhkam (unequivocal). When command intends permission or recommendation, some claimed it is a reality. Al-Karkhi and al-Jassas said: rather it is metaphorical; because the name of reality does not fluctuate between negation and affirmation. Since it is permissible to say: I am not commanded to transmit, that indicates it is metaphorical because its origin and transgression are permissible. The other view is that the meaning of permission and recommendation from obligation is part of it in estimation, as if it is deficient, not different; because obligation encompasses it. And this is more correct.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of command (amr) in Hanafi usul. The text begins by stating that the command does not require the addressee's choice for the ruling of obligation to apply; obligation is imposed by the command itself (al-amr lil-ijāb). However, the actual existence of the commanded act depends on the addressee's choice to avoid jabr (compulsion). Then it refutes the Wāqifiyya (those who suspend judgment on the meaning of command) by arguing that considering mere probability destroys all realities. It then presents the debate: some claim that command is haqiqah (real) for permission/ibāḥah or recommendation/nadb, but al-Karkhi and al-Jassas assert it is only haqiqah for obligation (wujūb), and other uses are metaphorical (majāz). The proof is that the term 'reality' does not tolerate negation; one can say 'I am not commanded' for a non-obligatory act, showing it is metaphorical. Finally, another view reconciles by saying permission/recommendation are part of obligation in effect but deficient.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): A verbal command that, in its original reality (haqiqah), indicates obligation (wujub); other uses (ibaha, nadb) are metaphorical (majaz).
الواقفية (al-wāqifiyya): Those who suspend judgment on the meaning of amr, claiming it only indicates probability, not certainty.
حقيقة (haqiqah): A word used in its original, literal meaning for which it was coined.
مجاز (majaz): A word used in a non-literal meaning due to a connection and context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is haqiqah (real).
B) It is majaz (metaphorical).
C) It is ambiguous (mujmal).
D) It is abrogated (mansukh).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a command (amr) is literally for obligation; any other meaning requires contextual evidence.

Defining Adaa' and Qadaa' in Hanafi Usul
Defining adaa' and qadaa' with subtypes and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define adaa' and qadaa' according to Hanafi usul, distinguish their three subtypes, and apply them to the example of usurpation (ghasb).

Core Idea
Adaa' is delivering the exact wajib; qadaa' is delivering its equivalent.

In this chapter, the author classifies the attributes of a command's ruling into two primary categories: performance (adaa') and replacement (qadaa'). These categories are fundamental to understanding how obligations are fulfilled in Hanafi fiqh, whether they pertain to the rights of Allah or the rights of servants. We will dissect the definitions and their subtypes, using a clear example from civil law (usurpation) and a Quranic verse to solidify the concepts.

Guided Path
1
State the two main categories
Identify adaa' and qadaa' as the two types of fulfilling a command. Memorize their definitions: adaa' is delivering the exact wajib; qadaa' is delivering an equivalent.
Note that these apply to both rights of Allah and rights of servants.
2
Identify the three types of adaa'
List the three subtypes: complete pure performance, deficient pure performance, and performance resembling qadaa'. Understand the nuance with examples like praying on time (complete) vs. praying without water in tayammum (deficient).
The third subtype, resembling qadaa', applies when an act is repeated due to a defect, e.g., redoing a prayer that had a flaw.
3
Identify the three types of qadaa'
List the three subtypes: with a conceivable equivalent (e.g., monetary compensation), with an inconceivable equivalent (e.g., acts of worship), and in the sense of adaa' (e.g., making up a missed fast).
The third type blurs the line: making up a fast is qadaa' but similar to adaa' because it is the same act performed later.
4
Apply the definitions to the ghasb example
Read the usurpation example: if the item still exists, returning it is adaa'; if it perishes, paying compensation is qadaa'. This shows the practical application.
This example is from private rights, illustrating that the categories apply outside worship.
5
Recognize the scope of adaa' and qadaa'
Note that adaa' can include supererogatory acts (nafl) according to those who view the command as covering permission and recommendation. In contrast, qadaa' never applies to nafl—only to obligatory acts.
This distinction is important for understanding the nature of commands in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب بيان صفة حكم الأمر وذلك نوعان : أداء، وقضاء. والأداء أنواع ثلاثةٌ: أداء كامل محض، وأداء قاصر محض، وما هو شبية بالقضاء. والقضاء أنواع ثلاثة : نوع بمثل معقول ونوع بمثل غير معقول، ونوع بمعنى الأداء. وهذه الأقسام تدخل في حقوق الله تعالى، وتدخل في حقوق العباد أيضاً. فالأداء : اسم لتسليم نفس الواجب بالأمر. والقضاء : اسم لتسليم مثل الواجب، به كمن غصب شيئاً: لزمه تسليم عينه، ورده فيصير به مؤدياً، وإذا هلك لزمه ضمانه، فيصير به قاضياً. وقد يدخل في الأداء قسم آخر، وهو النفل، على قول من جعل الأمر حقيقةً في الإباحة ،والندب فأما القضاء فلا يحتمل هذا الوصف. قال الله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْمُرُكُمْ أَن تُؤَدُّوا الْأَمَنتِ إِلَى أَهْلِهَا ﴾ النساء /٥٨ ، وإنه في تسليم أعيانها إلى أربابها. وقد تدخل إحدى العبارتين في قسم العبارة الأخرى، فيُسمى الأداء
Chapter: Explanation of the attribute of the command's ruling. That is of two types: performance (adaa') and replacement (qadaa'). And performance is of three types: complete pure performance, deficient pure performance, and what resembles replacement. And replacement is of three types: a type with a conceivable equivalent, a type with an inconceivable equivalent, and a type in the sense of performance. These divisions enter into the rights of Allah Most High and also enter into the rights of servants. So adaa' is a name for delivering the exact obligatory (act) by the command. And qadaa' is a name for delivering an equivalent of the obligatory act. For example, someone who usurps something: he is obligated to deliver the exact thing and return it, thereby becoming one who performs (mu'addiyan). If it perishes, he is obligated to guarantee it, thereby becoming one who replaces (qadiyan). And there may enter into adaa' another division, which is supererogatory (nafl), according to the one who makes the command real in permissibility and recommendation. As for qadaa', it does not admit of this description. Allah Most High said: 'Indeed, Allah commands you to render trusts to their owners' (4:58), and that is about delivering the exact things to their owners. And one of the two expressions may enter into the division of the other expression, so it is called adaa'.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage from the Hanafi usul text introduces a new chapter on the attributes of a command's ruling, focusing on the classification of performance (adaa') and replacement (qadaa'). The author defines adaa' as the delivery of the exact wajib (obligatory act) as commanded, and qadaa' as the delivery of its equivalent. Performance is subdivided into three: 1) complete pure performance (e.g., praying on time with all conditions), 2) deficient pure performance (e.g., praying in a state of minor impurity without water—valid but deficient), and 3) performance that resembles replacement (e.g., redoing a prayer due to a defect, which has elements of both). Replacement also has three types: 1) with a conceivable equivalent (e.g., a lost item's monetary value), 2) with an inconceivable equivalent (e.g., acts of worship like prayer—cannot be replaced with money but with similar acts), and 3) in the sense of performance (e.g., making up a missed fast—it is qadaa' but similar to adaa'). These categories apply both to rights of Allah (e.g., prayers, zakat) and rights of servants (e.g., debts, damages). The author gives the example of usurpation (ghasb): if the item exists, returning it is adaa'; if it perishes, paying compensation is qadaa'. The passage also notes that supererogatory acts (nafl) can be considered adaa' according to those who view the command as including permission and recommendation (e.g., Hanafi view on mandub). However, qadaa' never applies to supererogatory acts. The verse 4:58 is cited as evidence that adaa' involves delivering the exact trust. The final sentence indicates that sometimes the terms adaa' and qadaa' may overlap in usage.

Key Terms
الأداء (al-adaa'): The name for delivering the exact wajib (obligatory act) in accordance with the command.
القضاء (al-qadaa'): The name for delivering an equivalent of the wajib, not the exact act itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi definition of qadaa', which of the following is an example?
A) Praying on time with all conditions.
B) Giving charity voluntarily.
C) Paying compensation for a lost borrowed item.
D) Fasting on a recommended day.

Key Takeaway
Adaa' delivers the exact wajib; qadaa' delivers an equivalent—this distinction applies to both divine and human rights.
