Classification of Husn for Commanded Acts
Classifying commanded acts as good intrinsically or extrinsically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a commanded act as intrinsically good (ḥasan li-dhātihi) or extrinsically good (ḥasan li-ghayrihi) and identify the three subtypes within each category according to al-Bazdawi's taxonomy.

Core Idea
A commanded act's goodness may be intrinsic or extrinsic, unconditional or conditional—this determines its legal effect.

In this passage, Imam al-Bazdawi presents a detailed classification of the attribute of ḥusn (goodness) as applied to commanded acts (al-maʾmūr bihī). This taxonomy is essential for understanding why certain acts, though inherently good, can be set aside under specific circumstances, while others remain obligatory unconditionally. The distinction between intrinsic and extrinsic goodness forms the basis for subsequent discussions of obligation, recommendation, and exemption in Hanafi legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary categories of ḥusn
Commanded acts are divided into those good for a meaning in themselves (ḥasan li-dhātihi) and those good for a meaning in something else (ḥasan li-ghayrihi).
2
Examine the three subtypes of intrinsic goodness
Intrinsic goodness includes: (1) acts that never lose their goodness (e.g., taṣdīq), (2) acts that can lose it (e.g., iqrār), and (3) acts annexed to intrinsic goodness but resembling extrinsic goodness (e.g., ṣalāh).
3
Examine the three subtypes of extrinsic goodness
Extrinsic goodness includes: (1) acts good for an independent external goal, (2) acts where the benefit is realized through the act itself, and (3) the comprehensive (jāmiʿ) type—good for a condition after being intrinsically good.
4
Apply the classification to concrete examples
Identify which category and subtype apply to faith (taṣdīq and iqrār), prayer, zakāh, fasting, and ḥajj based on the text.
5
Understand the legal implications
The subtype determines whether an act's goodness can be removed (e.g., by duress, excuses) and whether it remains obligatory in all circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب بيان صفة الحُسْن للمأمور به المأمور به نوعان في هذا الباب: حَسَن لمعنى في نفسه، ولمعنى في غيره. فالحَسَنُ لمعنى في نفسه ثلاثة أضرب : ١- ضَرْبٌ لا يَقبل سقوط هذا الوصف بحال. ٢- وضَرْب يَقبله. ٣- وضَرْبٌ منه ملحق بهذا القسم، لكنه مشابه لما هو حَسَنٌ لمعنى في غيره. والذي هو حَسَنُ لمعنى في غيره ثلاثة أضرب أيضاً: ١- فضَرْبٌ منه : ما هو حَسَنٌ لمعنى في غيره، وذلك الغير قائم بنفسه مقصوداً، لا يتأدى بالذي قبله بحال. ٢- وضَرْبٌ منه : ما هو حَسَنُ لمعنى في غيره، لكنه يتأدى بنفس المأمور به فكان شبيهاً بالذي حَسَن لمعنى في نفسه. ٣- وضَرْبٌ منه ما هو حَسَنُ لحُسْنِ في شرطه بعد ما كان حَسَنَاً لمعنى في نفسه، أو ملحقاً به، وهذا القسم يُسمَّى جامعاً. وأما الضرب الأول من القسم الأول: فنحو الإيمان بالله تعالى، وصفاته : حَسَنُ لعينه، غير أنه نوعان: ١- تصديق : وهو ركن لا يحتمل السقوط بحال، حتى إنه متى تبدل بضده: كان كفراً. ٢- وإقرار : وهو ركن ملحق به لكنه يحتمل السقوط بحال، حتى إنه إذا تبدل بضده بعُذرِ الإكراه لم يُعَدَّ كفراً؛ لأن اللسان ليس معدن التصديق، لكن تَرْكُ البيان من غير عذر يدل على فوات التصديق، فكان ركناً، دون الأول. فمن صدق بقلبه ، وتَرَكَ البيان من غير عذر: لم يكن مؤمناً). ومن لم يصادف وقتاً يتمكن فيه من البيان، وكان مختاراً في التصديق: كان مؤمنا إن تحقق ذلك. والضرب الثاني من القسم الأول كالصلاة، حسنت لمعنى في نفسها من التعظيم الله تعالى، إلا أنها دون التصديق، وهي نظير الإقرار، حتى سقطت بأعذار كثيرة، إلا أنها ليست بركن في الإيمان، بخلاف الإقرار ؛ لأن في الإقرار وجوداً وعدماً دلالةً على التصديق. والضرب الثالث: الزكاة والصوم والحج، فإن الصوم صار حَسَناً لمعنى قهر النفس، والزكاة لمعنى دفع حاجة الفقير، والحج لمعنى شرف المكان، إلا أن هذه الوسائط غير مستحقة لأنفسها؛ لأن النفس ليست بجانية في صفتها، والفقير ليس بمستحق عبادة، والبيت ليس بمستحق (۱) ينظر كشف الأسرار للبخاري ١٨٥/١. سائد.
Chapter: Explanation of the attribute of goodness (al-ḥusn) for the commanded act. The commanded act is of two types in this chapter: good for a meaning in itself, and good for a meaning in something else. Good for a meaning in itself is of three kinds: 1- A kind that does not accept the removal of this description under any circumstance. 2- A kind that accepts its removal. 3- A kind annexed to this section, but similar to what is good for a meaning in something else. That which is good for a meaning in something else is also three kinds: 1- A kind that is good for a meaning in something else, and that something else subsists by itself, intended, and is not fulfilled by the former in any case. 2- A kind that is good for a meaning in something else, but it is fulfilled by the very same commanded act, so it resembles that which is good for a meaning in itself. 3- A kind that is good for a goodness in its condition after it was good for a meaning in itself or annexed to it; this section is called comprehensive (jāmiʿ). As for the first kind of the first category: it is like faith (īmān) in Allah and His attributes – good in itself. However, it is of two types: 1- Taṣdīq (inner conviction): It is a pillar (rukn) that does not admit removal under any circumstance, so if it changes to its opposite, it becomes disbelief. 2- Iqrār (verbal declaration): It is a pillar annexed to it, but it admits removal under a circumstance, such that if it changes to its opposite under duress, it is not counted as disbelief, because the tongue is not the locus of conviction. However, refraining from expression without excuse indicates the loss of conviction, so it is a pillar, but below the first. So whoever believes in his heart and refrains from expression without excuse is not a believer. And whoever does not find a time in which he is able to express, while freely choosing conviction, is a believer if that is verified. The second kind of the first category: like prayer (ṣalāh); it is good for a meaning in itself, namely glorifying Allah, but it is below conviction, and it is analogous to verbal declaration, so it can be waived by many excuses. However, it is not a pillar of faith, unlike verbal declaration, because in verbal declaration, its presence or absence indicates conviction. The third kind: zakāh, fasting, and ḥajj. Fasting becomes good for the meaning of subduing the soul, zakāh for the meaning of fulfilling the need of the poor, and ḥajj for the meaning of the honor of the place. However, these intermediate means are not deserving in themselves, because the soul is not transgressing in its description, the poor are not deserving of worship, and the House is not deserving (1) See Kashf al-Asrār by al-Bukhārī 1/185. End.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification by Imam al-Bazdawi of the attribute of 'goodness' (ḥusn) as it applies to the commanded acts (al-maʾmūr bihī) in Islamic law. The author divides commanded acts into two primary categories: those good for a meaning inherent in themselves, and those good for a meaning external to themselves. Each category is further subdivided into three types. The first category (good in itself) includes: (1) acts whose goodness cannot be removed under any circumstance, such as the inner conviction of faith (taṣdīq); (2) acts whose goodness can be removed under certain circumstances, such as the verbal declaration of faith (iqrār) which can be overridden by duress; (3) acts that are annexed to the first category but resemble those good for an external reason, such as prayer – though prayer is good in itself as an act of glorification, it can be set aside by many excuses. The second category (good for something else) includes: (1) acts good for an intended independent external goal that cannot be achieved without that act, e.g., zakāh for aiding the poor; (2) acts where the benefit is realized through the act itself, resembling intrinsic goodness; (3) acts that were intrinsically good or annexed to such but become good due to a condition, called comprehensive (jāmiʿ). The author then illustrates with detailed examples from faith and the pillars of Islam, clarifying that while the acts are good, their goodness may be conditional, subject to removal by excuses, or dependent on external factors. The classification helps students understand the varying degrees of obligation and the nature of the commanded acts.

Key Terms
الحُسْن (al-ḥusn): The attribute of goodness or beauty in a commanded act, which makes it commendable or obligatory; may be intrinsic or extrinsic.
المأمور به (al-maʾmūr bihī): That which is commanded by the Lawgiver; the act itself that is the object of a command.
جامع (jāmiʿ): A type of act that is good due to a condition (sharṭ) after having been good in itself or annexed to that; it combines both categories.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazdawi's classification, which of the following is an example of an act that is 'good for a meaning in itself' and does not accept the removal of its goodness under any circumstance?
A) Prayer (ṣalāh)
B) Fasting (ṣawm)
C) Inner conviction (taṣdīq) of faith
D) Zakāh

Key Takeaway
The classification of ḥusn into intrinsic and extrinsic, with conditional and unconditional subtypes, is foundational for determining the binding force of commanded acts in Hanafi usul.

Two Pillars of Iman: Tasdiq and Iqrar
Defines two pillars of iman and their legal characteristics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two pillars of iman according to the Hanafi school and distinguish between tasdiq and iqrar in terms of their susceptibility to falling.

Core Idea
Tasdiq never falls; iqrar can fall under duress because the tongue is not the seat of affirmation.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul defines the two components of iman according to the Hanafi school. Understanding their different legal characteristics is crucial for correctly assessing the status of a believer, especially in situations of coercion. The rule is a foundational principle in Hanafi usul that governs how we evaluate faith in the face of external pressures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pillars
List the two types of pillars mentioned: tasdiq (affirmation of the heart) and iqrar (verbal confession).
Both are considered 'rukn' but with different legal weight.
2
Determine the nature of each pillar
Note that tasdiq is an essential pillar (rukn asli) that never falls under any circumstance. Iqrar is an attached pillar (rukn mulhaq) that can fall under duress.
The author emphasizes that iqrar is not like the first; it is secondary.
3
State the consequence of each pillar changing
If tasdiq changes to its opposite (kufr), the person becomes an unbeliever. If iqrar changes under duress (ikrah), it is not considered kufr because the tongue is not the source of affirmation.
This distinction is key in cases of coercion.
4
Apply the rule to the scenario of omission
If a person affirms with the heart but deliberately abandons iqrar without excuse, they are not a believer. If they never had the opportunity to verbalize but freely choose to believe, they are a believer.
This shows that iqrar is normally required, but the requirement can be excused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصفاته : حَسَنُ لعينه ، غير أنه نوعان: ١- تصديق : وهو ركن لا يحتمل السقوط بحال، حتى إنه متى تبدل بضده: كان كفراً. ۲ - وإقرار : وهو ركن ملحق به لكنه يحتمل السقوط بحال، حتى إنه إذا تبدل بضده بعُذرِ الإكراه لم يُعَدَّ كفراً؛ لأن اللسان ليس معدن التصديق، لكن تَرْكُ البيان من غير عذر يدل على فوات التصديق، فكان ركناً، دون الأول. فمن صدق بقلبه ، وتَرَكَ البيان من غير عذر: لم يكن مؤمناً). ومن لم يصادف وقتاً يتمكن فيه من البيان، وكان مختاراً في التصديق: كان مؤمنا إن تحقق ذلك.
And its descriptions: good in itself, but it is of two types: 1- Affirmation: it is a pillar that does not admit falling under any circumstance, so that if it changes to its opposite, it becomes unbelief. 2- And Confession: it is a pillar attached to it, but it admits falling under some circumstance, so that if it changes to its opposite under the excuse of coercion, it is not considered unbelief; because the tongue is not the source of affirmation, but abandoning the declaration without excuse indicates the loss of affirmation, so it is a pillar, but not like the first. So whoever affirms with his heart and abandons the declaration without excuse is not a believer. And whoever does not find an opportunity in which he can declare, and is free in his affirmation, is a believer if that is verified.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the two components of iman according to the Hanafi school. The author, al-Bazdawi, explains that acts which are good in themselves (hasan li 'aynihi) include two types that constitute the pillars of iman. The first is tasdiq (affirmation of the heart), which is the essential pillar. It can never fall; if it turns to its opposite (kufr), the person becomes an unbeliever. The second is iqrar (verbal confession), which is an attached pillar. It can fall under duress because the tongue is not the seat of belief. However, deliberately abandoning iqrar without an excuse indicates that tasdiq is absent. Therefore, if a person believes in the heart but fails to verbalize it without valid excuse, he is not considered a believer. If he never had the opportunity to verbalize and freely chooses to believe, he is a believer.

Key Terms
تصديق (tasdiq): Affirmation of faith in the heart; the essential pillar of iman that never falls.
إقرار (iqrar): Verbal confession of faith; an attached pillar that can fall under duress.
ركن (rukn): A fundamental component; here tasdiq is a rukn that never drops, iqrar is a rukn 'mulhaq' (attached) that can drop.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a person is coerced into uttering words of kufr while his heart remains firm in iman?
A) He becomes a kafir because the tongue has spoken kufr.
B) He remains a believer because iqrar falls under duress.
C) He becomes a hypocrite.
D) His iman is suspended until he repents.

Key Takeaway
Tasdiq is the essential pillar that never falls; iqrar is an attached pillar that can fall under duress, but deliberate abandonment without excuse negates iman.

The Classification of Acts: Salah versus Pure Worship
Classifying salah and acts like zakat within the first division of husn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the classification of salah and acts like zakat, sawm, and hajj within the first division of acts good in themselves, and justify why salah is not a pillar of iman and why the latter are pure worship requiring full capacity.

Core Idea
Salah is good for glorification, but drops with excuses; zakat, sawm, hajj are pure worship because their means are not inherently deserving.

In Hanafi usul, the goodness (husn) of acts is classified. This passage from al-Bazdawi refines the first division (acts good in themselves) into three types: acts like iqrar, acts like salah, and acts like zakat. We focus on the latter two: salah, which is analogous to iqrar in falling with excuses but is not a pillar of iman, and the triad of zakat, fasting, and hajj, which are pure worship requiring complete legal capacity. Understanding this classification helps us derive rulings on when obligations apply.

Guided Path
1
Recall the broader classification
Acts are divided into those good in themselves (husn li-dhatihi) and those good for another. The first division is then subdivided. The passage presents the second and third types of the first division.
The first type (like iqrar) is not detailed here.
2
Analyze the second type: salah
Salah is good for the meaning of glorification (ta'zim). It is analogous to iqrar because both can fall away due to many excuses. However, unlike iqrar, salah does not serve as a direct indicator of tasdiq (affirmation of faith), so it is not a pillar of iman.
This distinction prevents equating practical obligation with foundational belief.
3
Analyze the third type: zakat, sawm, hajj
Each act has an external meaning that makes it good: fasting subdues the soul, zakat satisfies the poor's need, hajj honors the sacred place. However, these means (soul, poor, place) are not inherently deserving of worship. Hence, the acts are pure worship for Allah.
The means are mere intentions; the true object is obedience to Allah.
4
Derive the implication for legal capacity
Because these acts are pure worship, they require full legal capacity (ahliyyah kamilah) for obligation. This is why minors or those lacking sanity are not obliged to perform zakat, fasting, or hajj.
Salah, being similar to iqrar, may have different capacity requirements? The passage does not elaborate, but implies salah can fall with excuses even for those with capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضرب الثاني من القسم الأول كالصلاة، حسنت لمعنى في نفسها من التعظيم الله تعالى، إلا أنها دون التصديق، وهي نظير الإقرار، حتى سقطت بأعذار كثيرة، إلا أنها ليست بركن في الإيمان، بخلاف الإقرار ؛ لأن في الإقرار وجوداً وعدماً دلالةً على التصديق. والضرب الثالث: الزكاة والصوم والحج، فإن الصوم صار حَسَناً لمعنى قهر النفس، والزكاة لمعنى دفع حاجة الفقير، والحج لمعنى شرف المكان، إلا أن هذه الوسائط غير مستحقة لأنفسها؛ لأن النفس ليست بجانية في صفتها، والفقير ليس بمستحق عبادة، والبيت ليس بمستحق لنفسه، فصار هذا كالقسم الثاني عبادةً خالصة لله تعالى، حتى شرطنا لها أهلية كاملة. (۱) ينظر كشف الأسرار للبخاري ١٨٥/١. سائد.
And the second type of the first division: like prayer, it became good for a meaning in itself of glorifying Allah Almighty, but it is below affirmation, and it is analogous to confession, so that it falls away with many excuses, but it is not a pillar of faith, unlike confession; because in confession there is existence and non-existence as an indication of affirmation. And the third type: zakat, fasting, and pilgrimage, for fasting became good for the meaning of subduing the soul, and zakat for the meaning of fulfilling the need of the poor, and pilgrimage for the meaning of the honor of the place, but these means are not deserving in themselves; because the soul is not criminal in its attribute, the poor is not deserving of worship, and the house is not deserving in itself, so this, like the second division, became pure worship of Allah Almighty, until we required for it complete capacity.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of acts. The second type of the first division (acts good in themselves) includes salah. Salah is good because it contains the glorification of Allah, but it is below tasdiq and similar to iqrar in that it can be dropped due to many excuses (e.g., illness, travel). However, salah is not a pillar of iman, unlike iqrar, because iqrar directly indicates the presence or absence of tasdiq, whereas salah does not. The third type includes zakat, sawm (fasting), and hajj. These acts are good because of external meanings: fasting subdues the soul, zakat fulfills the need of the poor, and hajj honors the sacred place. The means themselves (soul, poor person, house) are not inherently deserving of worship. Hence, these acts are pure worship (ibadah khalisah) for Allah, requiring full capacity (ahliyyah kamilah) for their obligation.

Key Terms
الصلاة (al-salah): Prayer; an act good for glorifying Allah, but not a pillar of iman; can fall with excuses.
عبادة خالصة (ibadah khalisah): Pure worship directed solely to Allah, requiring full legal capacity.
أهلية كاملة (ahliyyah kamilah): Complete legal capacity: sanity, puberty, and ability to perform the act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both are pillars of iman.
B) Both can be dropped due to many excuses (sutta bi-a'dhar kathira).
C) Both involve glorification of Allah.
D) Both require complete legal capacity.

Key Takeaway
Salah is good for glorification and drops with excuses like iqrar, but it is not a pillar of iman; zakat, sawm, and hajj are pure worship requiring full capacity because their external means are not inherently deserving.

Acts Good as Means: The Hanafi Concept of Wasila
Explains acts good as means with examples and a hadith rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish acts that are good as means (wasila) from acts that are good in themselves, and apply the Hanafi principle to determine when intention is required.

Core Idea
An act can be good not for itself but as a means to a higher end — intention is then not required for validity.

In Hanafi usul, not all acts are valued for their own sake. Some derive their goodness from being a means to another valid goal. This passage from al-Pazdawi uses four examples: going to Friday prayer, wudu, jihad, and funeral prayer. Understanding this distinction helps us see why the Hanafi school sometimes dispenses with intention (niyyah) and how acts are contingent on external causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division of acts
The text discusses the 'first type of the second division' — acts that are good as means, not ends. Locate this classification and note how it contrasts with acts good in themselves.
The full classification is not given here, but the focus is on means-based acts.
2
Analyze the first subtype: sa'y and wudu
Examine the examples of going to Friday prayer (sa'y) and wudu. The text says they are not intended obligations or devotions per se; they are good for enabling something else (Friday prayer, regular prayer). How does the text argue that wudu is valid without intention?
Hanafi position: wudu for purification does not require niyyah; as a devotional act (qurbah), it does require intention if reward is sought.
3
Analyze the second subtype: jihad and funeral prayer
These are good because of an external meaning: disbelief in the case of jihad, and Islam of the deceased for funeral prayer. They are contingent — if the cause disappears, the act would no longer be legislated.
The text raises a hypothetical: if all disbelievers become Muslim, jihad would end. But it says this contradicts the hadith.
4
Understand the hadith response
The text cites a hadith that a group will fight until the end, so the hypothetical scenario cannot occur. This shows how a textual evidence (khabar) can override a theoretical extrapolation.
The hadith is from 'Imran ibn Husain and is recorded by Nasa'i and Abu Dawud.
5
Extract the underlying usul principle
The common thread: an act is evaluated not by its intrinsic nature but by its role as a means to a shari'ah goal. This affects rulings like the necessity of intention, the persistence of obligation, and the scope of the act.
This principle is used in many Hanafi rulings, e.g., wudu without intention, sa'y not being fard itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الضرب الأول من القسم الثاني : فمثل السعي إلى الجمعة، ليس بفرض مقصود، إنما حَسُن لإقامة الجمعة؛ لأن العبد به يتمكن إقامة الجمعة، وقد لا تتأدى به الجمعة. وكذلك الوضوء عندنا، من حيث هو فعل يفيد الطهارة للبدن: ليس بعبادة مقصودة؛ لأنه في نفسه تَبرُّدُّ وتَطهر ، لكن إنما حَسُن؛ لأنه يُراد به إقامة الصلاة، ولا تتأدى به الصلاة بحال، ويَسقط بسقوطها، ويُستغنى عن صفة القُربة في الوضوء، حتى يصح من غير نية عندنا. ومن حيث جُعِلَ الوضوء في الشرع قُربةً يُراد بها ثواب الآخرة كسائر القرب: لا تتأدى بغير نية إلا أن الصلاة تستغني عن هذا الوصف في الوضوء. والضرب الثاني: الجهاد وصلاة الجنازة إنما صارا حَسَنَيْن لمعنى كفر الكافر، وإسلام الميت، وذلك معنى منفصل عن الجهاد والصلاة، حتى إن الكفار إن أسلموا لم يبق الجهاد مشروعاً إن تُصور ذلك، لكنه خلاف الخَبَر (۱) ، وإذا صار حقُّ المسلم مقضياً بصلاة البعض: سقط عن أن (۱) باب بيان صفة الحُسن : قوله : لكنه خلاف الخبر : عن عمران بن حصين رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: لا تزال طائفة من أمتي يقاتلون على الحق، ظاهرين على من ناوأهم حتى يقاتل آخرهم المسيح الدجال». أخرجه النسائي وأبو داود.
And as for the first type of the second division: such as going to Friday prayer, it is not an intended obligation; rather it is good for establishing Friday prayer, because by it the servant is able to establish Friday prayer, and Friday prayer may not be accomplished by it. And similarly, wudu according to us, insofar as it is an act that provides physical purification, is not an intended act of worship, because in itself it is cooling and cleansing, but it is good because it is intended for the establishment of prayer, and prayer is never accomplished by it, and it falls away with the falling away of prayer, and the attribute of devotion in wudu is dispensed with, so that it is valid without intention according to us. And insofar as wudu has been made in the Shari'ah a devotion intended for the reward of the Hereafter like other devotions, it is not accomplished without intention, except that prayer dispenses with this description in wudu. And the second type: jihad and funeral prayer, they became good for the meaning of the disbelief of the disbeliever and the submission of the deceased, and that is a meaning separate from jihad and prayer, such that if the disbelievers become Muslims, jihad would not remain legislated if that could be imagined, but that contradicts the hadith (1), and when the right of the Muslim is fulfilled by the prayer of some, it falls from others. (1) Section on the description of goodness: his saying: but it contradicts the hadith: from 'Imran ibn Husain, the Messenger of Allah (may peace be upon him) said: 'A group of my ummah will continue to fight for the truth, dominant over those who oppose them, until the last of them fights the Dajjal.' Narrated by Nasa'i and Abu Dawud.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage discusses the second division of acts: those that are good not for themselves but as means to other ends. The first type includes going to Friday prayer (sa'y al-jumu'ah), which is not an obligatory end in itself but is good for establishing Friday prayer. Similarly, wudu in its physical aspect is not an act of worship; it is merely cooling and cleansing. It is valid without intention (niyyah) according to the Hanafi school because the intention of worship is not required for its validity; rather, it is a means to prayer. However, wudu as a devotional act (qurbah) does require intention if one seeks reward. The second type includes jihad and funeral prayer. Jihad is good because it aims to combat disbelief, and funeral prayer is good because it benefits the deceased Muslim. These acts are contingent on external conditions; if the reason disappears (e.g., all disbelievers embrace Islam), the act would no longer be legislated, but that contradicts the hadith that a group will fight until the end. The footnote explains this hadith and its implication.

Key Terms
السعي إلى الجمعة (al-sa'yu ila al-jumu'ah): Going to Friday prayer; an act that is good as a means to establish the Friday prayer, not an intended obligation.
الوضوء (al-wudu): Ablution; an act that in itself is physical cleansing, but made a devotional act for prayer. According to Hanafis, it is valid without intention as a means.
الجهاد (al-jihad): Struggle against disbelief; an act good for the meaning of combating kufr, not for itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of going to Friday prayer (sa'y)?
A) It is an intended obligation (fard maqsud).
B) It is good as a means to establish Friday prayer, not an obligation in itself.
C) It is a sunnah act that has no connection to Friday prayer.
D) It is an act that is purely physical cleansing like wudu.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, acts that are good as means (wasila) derive their value from the ends they serve, and intention is not required for their validity unless performed as a purely devotional act.

The Three Subtypes of Externally Good Acts (Qism Thani) in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the three subtypes of the second category of morally good acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three subtypes of acts that are good (hasan) due to external purposes and apply the Hanafi distinction between ability as a condition for performance (shart al-ada') versus obligation (shart al-wujub).

Core Idea
Acts good for external reasons divide into means, external cause, and capacity to perform; ability conditions performance, not obligation.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Bazdawi unpacks the second category of morally good acts — those good not inherently, but for an external purpose. The author provides three distinct subtypes: acts that are good because they facilitate an obligatory act (like going to Friday prayer), acts good due to a separate external cause (like jihad because of disbelief), and the concept of ability (qudra) which is a condition for performing an obligation, not for the obligation itself. The Quranic verse 2:286 is cited as proof for the third subtype. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly deriving rulings on when an act is required, recommended, or excused due to incapacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three subtypes
From the passage, list the three subdivisions of the second category (qism thani) of acts that are hasan (morally good). The author explicitly names them and gives examples for each.
Focus on the terms: al-darb al-awwal, al-darb al-thani, al-darb al-thalith.
2
Understand the basis for each subtype
For the first subtype (going to Friday prayer, wudu), note that the act is good because it is a means (wasila) to establish another worship. For the second subtype (jihad, funeral prayer), the goodness comes from an external cause (kufr of the disbeliever, Islam of the deceased). For the third subtype, the goodness is not in the act itself but in the capacity (qudra) that enables performance.
The third subtype is not an act per se but a legal condition. The author highlights that it is a condition for performance (shart al-ada') not obligation (shart al-wujub).
3
Analyze the proof for the third subtype
The Quranic verse 2:286 is presented as the basis for the rule that ability is a condition for performance. Examine how the verse is used: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.' The author derives that the obligation remains, but the requirement to perform is lifted when one lacks ability.
This is a key Hanafi distinction. Other schools may interpret the verse as making ability a condition for obligation itself.
4
Apply the distinction to a new example
Take the case of a person who is ill and cannot use water for wudu without worsening their condition. Using the third subtype, determine whether the obligation of wudu remains and whether the inability to perform absolves the person.
The passage mentions 'one unable to use water due to illness' as an example of where ability is a condition.
5
Contrast with other schools
Consider how the Mutakallimun (Ash'ari) would view the same verse. They typically argue that ability is a condition for the obligation itself (shart al-wujub), meaning the obligation does not arise if one lacks capacity. This difference has practical implications for fiqh rulings.
The Hanafi position is unique in separating the command from the ability to fulfill it, allowing the obligation to be established in principle while excusing performance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنفسه، فصار هذا كالقسم الثاني عبادةً خالصة لله تعالى، حتى شرطنا لها أهلية كاملة. وأما الضرب الأول من القسم الثاني : فمثل السعي إلى الجمعة، ليس بفرض مقصود، إنما حَسُن لإقامة الجمعة؛ لأن العبد به يتمكن إقامة الجمعة، وقد لا تتأدى به الجمعة. وكذلك الوضوء عندنا، من حيث هو فعل يفيد الطهارة للبدن: ليس بعبادة مقصودة؛ لأنه في نفسه تَبرُّدُّ وتَطهر ، لكن إنما حَسُن؛ لأنه يُراد به إقامة الصلاة، ولا تتأدى به الصلاة بحال، ويَسقط بسقوطها، ويُستغنى عن صفة القُربة في الوضوء، حتى يصح من غير نية عندنا. ومن حيث جُعِلَ الوضوء في الشرع قُربةً يُراد بها ثواب الآخرة كسائر القرب: لا تتأدى بغير نية إلا أن الصلاة تستغني عن هذا الوصف في الوضوء. والضرب الثاني: الجهاد وصلاة الجنازة إنما صارا حَسَنَيْن لمعنى كفر الكافر، وإسلام الميت، وذلك معنى منفصل عن الجهاد والصلاة، حتى إن الكفار إن أسلموا لم يبق الجهاد مشروعاً إن تُصور ذلك، لكنه خلاف الخَبَر (١) باب بيان صفة الحُسن : قوله : لكنه خلاف الخبر : عن عمران بن حصين رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: لا تزال طائفة من أمتي يقاتلون على الحق، ظاهرين على من ناوأهم حتى يقاتل آخرهم المسيح الدجال». أخرجه النسائي وأبو داود. الباقين، ولما كان المقصود يتأدى بالمأمور به بعينه كان شبيهاً بالقسم الأول. وأما الضرب الثالث: فمختص بالأداء دون القضاء، وذلك عبارة عن القدرة التي يتمكن بها العبد من أداء ما لزمه، وذلك شرط الأداء، دون الوجوب. وأصل ذلك : قول الله تعالى: ﴿ لَا يُكَلِّفُ اللهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا ﴾ (البقرة / ٢٨٦). وهو نوعان: مطلق، وكامل. فأما المطلق منه: فأدنى ما يتمكن به المأمور من أداء ما لزمه بدنياً كان أو مالياً، وهذا فضل ومنةٌ من الله تعالى عندنا. وهذا شرط في أداء حكم كل أمر حتى أجمعوا أن الطهارة بالماء لا تجب على العاجز عنها ببدنه، وعلى مَن عَجَزَ عن استعماله إلا بنقصان يَحلّ به، أو بماله في الزيادة على ثمن مثله، وفي مرض يزداد به.
For itself, so this became like the second category: a pure act of worship for Allah Almighty, so we stipulated for it complete capacity. As for the first subtype of the second category: such as going to Friday prayer, it is not an intended obligation, but it is good for establishing Friday prayer; because the servant through it can establish Friday prayer, and he may not fulfill Friday prayer through it. Similarly, ablution with us, from the aspect that it is an act that provides purification for the body: it is not an intended act of worship; because in itself it is cooling and purification, but it is good because it is intended for establishing prayer, and prayer is not fulfilled by it at all, and it drops with its dropping, and the attribute of proximity is dispensed with in ablution, so it is valid without intention with us. And from the aspect that ablution is made in law a act of proximity intended for the reward of the Hereafter like other acts of proximity: it is not fulfilled without intention, except that prayer does not need this attribute in ablution. The second subtype: jihad and funeral prayer became good for the meaning of disbelief of the disbeliever and Islam of the deceased, and that meaning is separate from jihad and prayer, to the point that if the disbelievers become Muslim, jihad would not remain legislated if that could be imagined, but it contradicts the hadith. [Footnote: This is a chapter explaining the attribute of goodness: his saying 'but it contradicts the report': from Imran ibn Husayn: the Messenger of Allah (SAW) said: 'A group of my ummah will continue to fight for the truth, dominant over those who oppose them until the last of them fights the Dajjal.' Narrated by Nasa'i and Abu Dawud.] The rest, and when the intended purpose is fulfilled by the commanded act itself, it became similar to the first category. As for the third subtype: it is specific to performance not make-up, and that is the ability by which the servant can perform what is obligatory upon him, and that is a condition for performance, not for obligation. And its origin is the saying of Allah Almighty: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (Quran 2:286). And it is of two types: absolute and complete. As for the absolute: it is the minimum by which the commanded person can perform what is obligatory upon him, whether bodily or financial, and this is a favor and grace from Allah Almighty with us. And this is a condition in the performance of every legal rule until they agreed that purification with water is not obligatory upon one unable to use it with his body, or upon one who is unable to use it except through a defect that affects him, or with his wealth in excess of its equivalent price, or in a disease that increases thereby.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is from the section on moral value (husn) of acts. The author, al-Bazdawi, is explaining the three subtypes of the second category of acts that are good (hasan). The second category refers to acts that are good due to an external purpose, not inherently. The first subtype includes acts like walking to Friday prayer and wudu (ablution). These are not obligatory in themselves but are good because they facilitate the establishment of Friday prayer or prayer respectively. The second subtype includes jihad and funeral prayer, which are good because of an external cause: the disbelief of the disbeliever or the Islam of the deceased. The author notes that if disbelievers all became Muslim, jihad would cease to be legislated, but this contradicts a hadith about continuous jihad. The third subtype relates to ability (qudra), which is a condition for performance, not obligation. The author cites Quran 2:286 and divides ability into absolute (minimum to perform) and complete. Absolute ability is a condition for every command, and the author gives examples like the obligation of purification with water being lifted for those unable to use it.

Key Terms
قسم ثاني (qism thānī): Acts that are good (hasan) not in themselves but for an external purpose, such as establishing a worship or achieving a benefit.
حسن (ḥusn): Moral goodness of an act; here applied to acts that are praiseworthy in Islamic law due to their purpose.
قربة (qurba): An act that brings one closer to Allah and is intended for reward in the afterlife.
القدرة (al-qudra): Ability or capacity to perform an obligatory act; a condition for performance (shart al-ada') not for obligation (shart al-wujub).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which subtype of the second category includes acts that are good due to an external cause, such as the disbelief of the disbeliever?
A) The first subtype (going to Friday prayer)
B) The second subtype (jihad and funeral prayer)
C) The third subtype (ability)
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, acts of the second category are good for an external reason, and the third subtype establishes that ability is a condition for performing an obligation, not for the obligation itself.

The Third Type of Capacity: Quwwat al-Ada' (Capacity for Performance)
Defining and applying capacity for performance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between capacity for obligation and capacity for performance, apply the Quranic proof to determine when capacity is required, and evaluate the Zufar disagreement using the principle of istihsan.

Core Idea
Capacity for performance is a condition for performing an obligation, not for its initial obligation—possibility of capacity suffices for obligation.

In Hanafi usul, capacity (qudra) is not a monolithic concept. This lesson unpacks the third type: capacity for performance (qudrat al-ada'), which is a condition for the valid performance of an already obligatory act, not for the act's initial obligation. The primary evidence is Q. 2:286, and the passage explores its implications, including a nuanced dispute with Zufar about whether obligation requires enough time for the full act or just a part.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of capacity mentioned
The passage divides capacity for performance into absolute (minimal) and complete. Understand the difference: absolute is the bare minimum to perform; complete includes extras like provision for Hajj.
Both are conditions for performance, not obligation.
2
Locate the Quranic dalil and its legal implication
Find the verse (Q. 2:286) and note how it is used to justify that capacity is a condition. The phrase 'لا يكلف الله نفسا إلا وسعها' is general, but the Hanafis interpret it to mean capacity at the time of performance, not obligation.
The verse is qat'i al-thubut and qat'i al-dalala.
3
Analyze the Zufar dispute and the Hanafi response via istihsan
Zufar argued that a woman after menses, etc., must catch enough time for the full prayer to be obligated. The Hanafis (using istihsan) said catching a part of the time for the opening takbir suffices, because the cause of obligation is the entry of time, and possibility of capacity is enough.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis obligate Maghrib if menses end just before sunset; Zufar does not.
4
Apply the principle to transfer to substitute (tayammum)
The passage ends with the rule: present incapacity triggers transfer to a prescribed substitute (e.g., tayammum). However, the original obligation was established because possibility of capacity existed at the time of the cause.
This shows how capacity for performance is distinct from capacity for obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الضرب الثالث: فمختص بالأداء دون القضاء، وذلك عبارة عن القدرة التي يتمكن بها العبد من أداء ما لزمه، وذلك شرط الأداء، دون الوجوب. وأصل ذلك : قول الله تعالى: ﴿ لَا يُكَلِّفُ اللهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا ﴾ [البقرة / ٢٨٦]. وهو نوعان: مطلق، وكامل. فأما المطلق منه: فأدنى ما يتمكن به المأمور من أداء ما لزمه بدنياً كان أو مالياً، وهذا فضل ومنةٌ من الله تعالى عندنا. وهذا شرط في أداء حكم كل أمر حتى أجمعوا أن الطهارة بالماء لا تجب على العاجز عنها ببدنه، وعلى مَن عَجَزَ عن استعماله إلا بنقصان يَحلّ به، أو بماله في الزيادة على ثمن مثله، وفي مرض يزداد به. وكذلك الصلاة لا يجب أداؤها إلا بهذه القدرة، والحج لا يجب أداؤه إلا بالزاد والراحلة؛ لأن تمكن السفر المخصوص به لا يحصل بدونهما في الغالب، ولا تجب الزكاة إلا بقدرة مالية، حتى إذا هلك النصاب بعد الحول قبل التمكن من الأداء سقط الواجب، بالإجماع. ولهذا قال زفر رحمه الله : في المرأة تطهر من حيضها أو نفاسها، أو الكافِرِ يسلم، أو الصبي يبلغ في آخر الوقت أن لا صلاة عليهم إلا أن يُدركوا وقتاً صالحاً للأداء؛ لما قلنا. لكن أصحابنا رحمهم الله استحسنوا بعد تمام الحيض، أو دلالة انقطاعه قبل تمامه بإدراك وقت الغُسل : أنها تجب بإدراك جزء يسير من الوقت يصلح للإحرام بها. وكذلك في سائر الفصول؛ لأنا نحتاج إلى سبب الوجوب، وذلك جزء من الوقت ونحتاج لوجوب الأداء إلى احتمال وجود القدرة، لا إلى تحقق القدرة وجوداً؛ لأن ذلك شرط حقيقة الأداء، فأما سابقاً عليه : فلا؛ لأنها لا تسبق الفعل إلا في الأسباب والآلات، لكن توهم القدرة يكفي لوجوب الأصل مشروعاً. ثم العجز الحالي دليلُ النقل إلى البدل المشروع عند فوات الأصل، وقد وجد احتمال القدرة باحتمال امتداد الوقت عن الجزء الأخير بوقف الشمس، كما كان لسليمان صلى الله عليه وسلم، وذلك نظير السماء، فصار مشروعاً، ثم وجب النقل إلى البدل؛ للعجز الحالي. كمَن هجم عليه وقت الصلاة وهو في السفر : أن خطاب الأصل يتوجه عليه؛ لاحتمال وجود الماء، ثم بالعجز الحالي : ينتقل إلى التراب.
As for the third type: it is specific to performance (ada') without making up (qada'), and it is the capacity by which the servant is able to perform what is incumbent upon him, and it is a condition for performance, not for obligation. The basis for this is the saying of Allah Most High: "Allah does not charge a soul except [with that within] its capacity" [al-Baqarah 2:286]. And it is of two types: absolute and complete. As for the absolute: it is the minimum by which the commanded person is able to perform what is incumbent upon him, whether bodily or financial, and this is a favor and grace from Allah Most High in our view. And this is a condition for performing every command, to the extent that they have agreed that purification with water is not obligatory for one who is incapable of it physically, or for one who cannot use it except with harm befalling him, or with his wealth exceeding its fair price, or in an illness that increases thereby. Similarly, prayer is not obligatory to perform except with this capacity, and Hajj is not obligatory to perform except with provision and mount, because the ability for the specific travel is usually not achieved without them, and Zakat is not obligatory except with financial capacity, to the extent that if the nisab perishes after the year before the ability to pay, the obligation falls, by consensus. And for this reason, Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him) said regarding a woman who becomes pure from her menses or postpartum bleeding, or a disbeliever who accepts Islam, or a child who reaches puberty at the end of the time, that there is no prayer upon them unless they catch a time suitable for performance, because of what we said. But our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) deemed it preferable after the completion of menses, or the indication of its cessation before completion by catching the time of ghusl, that it becomes obligatory by catching a small part of the time suitable for beginning the prayer. And similarly in all other chapters, because we need a cause for obligation, and that is a part of time, and we need for the obligation of performance the possibility of capacity, not the actual existence of capacity, because that is a condition for the reality of performance. As for what precedes it, no, because capacity does not precede the action except in causes and instruments, but the presumption of capacity suffices for the obligation of the original act as prescribed. Then present incapacity is evidence for transfer to the prescribed substitute when the original is lost, and the possibility of capacity has been found by the possibility of the time extending beyond the last part by the stopping of the sun, as was the case for Sulayman (peace be upon him), and that is similar to the sky, so it becomes prescribed, then transfer to the substitute becomes obligatory due to present incapacity. For example, one upon whom the time of prayer arrives while he is traveling: the address of the original applies to him due to the possibility of finding water, then due to present incapacity, he transfers to earth (tayammum).
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the third type of capacity (qudra) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author distinguishes between capacity for obligation (wujub) and capacity for performance (ada'). The third type is capacity for performance alone, not for making up (qada'). It is the ability to perform an already obligatory act. The primary textual proof is Quran 2:286, which establishes that Allah does not impose burdens beyond capacity. This capacity is divided into two: absolute (the bare minimum to perform) and complete. Absolute capacity is a prerequisite for performing any command—whether physical (like prayer) or financial (like zakat). The author provides examples: purification with water is not required if one is physically unable or can only obtain it at excessive cost or harm; prayer requires the capacity to perform it; Hajj requires provision and transportation; zakat requires financial capacity such that if the nisab perishes before payment, the obligation drops. A key furu' (branch) involves Zufar's opinion that if a woman's menses end, a disbeliever accepts Islam, or a child reaches puberty at the very end of the prayer time, they are not obligated to pray unless they catch enough time to perform the prayer. The Hanafi school (our companions) differed, ruling that it suffices to catch a part of the time suitable for the opening takbir, because the cause (sabab) of obligation is the entry of time, and for performance, the possibility of capacity suffices—actual existence is not required before the action. The passage concludes with the rule that present incapacity triggers transfer to a substitute (like tayammum when water is unavailable), but the possibility of capacity at the time of obligation initiation keeps the original obligation intact until incapacity manifests.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra): Capacity or power to perform an act of worship (ibaadah).
شرط الأداء (shart al-ada'): A condition for the valid performance of an obligatory act, not for its initial obligation.
البدل المشروع (al-badal al-mashru'): The legally prescribed substitute for an original act when the latter cannot be performed due to incapacity (e.g., tayammum for wudu).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary evidence for the distinction between capacity for performance and capacity for obligation?
A) The hadith 'Actions are by intentions'
B) The Quranic verse 'Allah does not charge a soul except its capacity'
C) Ijma' of the companions
D) Qiyas on the obligation of Hajj

Key Takeaway
Capacity for performance is a condition for performing a wajib, not for its initial obligation—the possibility of capacity suffices to establish the duty.

Obligation Requires Capacity and Valid Time
Explains that obligatory acts require specific capacity and a valid time portion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between the cause of obligation (sabab al-wujub) and the condition for performance (shart al-ada') and apply it to the case of a woman attaining purity at the end of prayer time.

Core Idea
Obligation to perform an act requires only a valid cause and possibility of capacity, not actual capacity.

This passage addresses a fundamental usul principle: what does it take for an obligation to become binding for performance? Hanafis distinguish between the cause that triggers the obligation (like entering prayer time) and the capacity needed to actually perform. The key insight: actual capacity is not required for obligation to arise; only the possibility of capacity suffices. This leads to important practical differences in cases like a woman who becomes pure at the very end of Asr time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main examples
The passage uses prayer, Hajj, and zakat to illustrate capacity requirements. List each and note the specific capacity needed (e.g., purity for prayer, provision and mount for Hajj, financial capacity for zakat).
These examples show that capacity is a condition for performance, not for the original obligation.
2
Locate the dispute with Zufar
What is the issue regarding those who become obligated at the end of time? State Zufar's view (requires full time for performance) and the Hanafi view (a small portion of time suffices).
Imam Zufar's view is more restrictive; Hanafis use istihsan to extend obligation.
3
Extract the usul principle
The Hanafi response hinges on the role of time as cause and capacity as condition. Identify the specific phrases: 'جزء من الوقت' (portion of time) as cause, and 'احتمال وجود القدرة' (possibility of capacity) as sufficient for obligation.
This distinction is key: the cause triggers obligation; capacity is only needed for actual performance.
4
Understand the istihsan argument
The Hanafi masters used istihsan to rule that a small portion of time suffices. Note how they prioritize the cause over the condition. The possibility of time extension (like for Prophet Sulaiman) supports the idea that the obligation was originally valid.
Istihsan is a Hanafi method that allows departure from strict qiyas for a stronger reason.
5
Apply the principle to the travel case
The passage extends the principle: if water is available at the start but lost later, the original obligation (wudu) remains, but inability transfers to tayammum. This shows the distinction between original obligation and substitute.
Current inability does not negate the original obligation; it merely shifts to a substitute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك الصلاة لا يجب أداؤها إلا بهذه القدرة، والحج لا يجب أداؤه إلا بالزاد والراحلة؛ لأن تمكن السفر المخصوص به لا يحصل بدونهما في الغالب، ولا تجب الزكاة إلا بقدرة مالية، حتى إذا هلك النصاب بعد الحول قبل التمكن من الأداء سقط الواجب، بالإجماع.
ولهذا قال زفر رحمه الله : في المرأة تطهر من حيضها أو نفاسها، أو الكافِرِ يسلم، أو الصبي يبلغ في آخر الوقت أن لا صلاة عليهم إلا أن يُدركوا وقتاً صالحاً للأداء؛ لما قلنا.
لكن أصحابنا رحمهم الله استحسنوا بعد تمام الحيض، أو دلالة انقطاعه قبل تمامه بإدراك وقت الغُسل : أنها تجب بإدراك جزء يسير من الوقت يصلح للإحرام بها.
وكذلك في سائر الفصول ؛ لأنا نحتاج إلى سبب الوجوب، وذلك جزء من الوقت ونحتاج لوجوب الأداء إلى احتمال وجود القدرة، لا إلى تحقق القدرة وجوداً؛ لأن ذلك شرط حقيقة الأداء، فأما سابقاً عليه : فلا؛ لأنها لا تسبق الفعل إلا في الأسباب والآلات، لكن توهم القدرة يكفي لوجوب الأصل مشروعاً.
ثم العجز الحالي دليلُ النقل إلى البدل المشروع عند فوات الأصل، وقد وجد احتمال القدرة باحتمال امتداد الوقت عن الجزء الأخير بوقف الشمس، كما كان لسليمان صلى الله عليه وسلم، وذلك نظير السماء، فصار مشروعاً، ثم وجب النقل إلى البدل؛ للعجز الحالي. كمَن هجم عليه وقت الصلاة وهو في السفر : أن خطاب الأصل يتوجه وجود الماء، ثم بالعجز الحالي : ينتقل إلى التراب.
عليه؛ لاحتمال
Similarly, prayer is not obligatory to perform except with this capacity, and Hajj is not obligatory to perform except with provision and mount; because the ability for the specific journey is not usually obtained without them. Zakat is not obligatory except with financial capacity, such that if the nisab is destroyed after the year before ability to deliver, the obligation falls, by consensus.
For this reason, Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him) said regarding a woman who becomes pure from her menses or postpartum bleeding, or an infidel who accepts Islam, or a boy who reaches puberty at the end of the time: that no prayer is upon them unless they catch a valid time for performance; because of what we said.
But our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) deemed it good after completion of menses, or indication of its cessation before completion by reaching the time of ghusl: that it becomes obligatory by catching a small portion of time suitable for takbirat al-ihram.
And similarly in all other chapters; because we need a cause for obligation, and that is a portion of time, and we need for obligation of performance the possibility of the existence of capacity, not the actual existence of capacity; because that is a condition of the reality of performance. As for before it, no; because it [capacity] does not precede the act except in causes and instruments, but the presumption of capacity is sufficient for the obligation of the originally prescribed [act].
Then current inability is evidence of transfer to the prescribed substitute when the original is lost, and the possibility of capacity has been found by the possibility of extension of time from the last portion by the stopping of the sun, as was for Sulaiman (peace be upon him), and that is similar to the sky [or: sun], so it became prescribed, then transfer to the substitute became obligatory due to current inability. Like one upon whom the time of prayer enters while he is traveling: the original address is directed to the existence of water, then due to current inability he transfers to earth.
Upon him; for possibility.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage discusses a usul rule: obligation to perform an act requires both a valid cause (sabab) and capacity (qudra). The author uses examples from prayer, Hajj, and zakat. For prayer, it is not obligatory to perform without capacity (e.g., purity, health). For Hajj, it requires provision and mount. For zakat, financial capacity is needed; if the nisab perishes after the year but before ability to deliver, the obligation drops by consensus.

Then a dispute is mentioned: Imam Zufar holds that a woman after menses, a convert, or a boy reaching puberty at the very end of the prayer time has no obligation unless they catch a time span sufficient for the entire prayer. However, the Hanafi masters (Ashabuna) ruled by istihsan that obligation arises by catching even a small portion of time sufficient for the opening takbir (tahrimah). This is based on the principle that the cause of obligation is a portion of the time, and for the obligation to perform, only the possibility of capacity (not actual capacity) is needed. Capacity is a condition for actual performance; it does not precede the act except in causes and means. Thus, the mere presumption of capacity suffices for the obligation of the originally prescribed act.

Further, current inability leads to transfer to a substitute. The example: if one is traveling and water is available at the beginning of time but then is lost, he transfers to tayammum. The possibility of capacity is established by the possibility that time could extend (e.g., sun standing still for Prophet Sulaiman), making the original act prescribed. Then due to current inability, transfer to substitute becomes obligatory.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra): Capacity or ability to perform an act, which is a condition for the obligation of performance (wujub al-ada') but not for the obligation itself (wujub asli).
جزء من الوقت (juz' min al-waqt): A portion of the prayer time sufficient for takbirat al-ihram, which is the minimal cause for obligation of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a woman who becomes pure from menses at the end of Asr time become obliged to pray Asr according to the Hanafi position?
A) Only if she has time to perform the entire prayer before sunset.
B) When she catches even a small part of the time sufficient for takbirat al-ihram.
C) She is not obliged at all because the time has almost passed.
D) She must pray Qada' only.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, obligation to perform an act arises from a valid cause (e.g., time portion) and the possibility of capacity, not actual capacity; current inability transfers to a substitute.

Qudrat al-Muyassira: Easing Capacity and Its Effect on Obligations
Defining the second type of capacity and its effect on obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between enabling capacity (qudrat al-tamkin) and easing capacity (qudrat al-muyassira) and apply the rule that obligations depending on easing capacity drop when the capacity ceases.

Core Idea
Easing capacity changes the obligation's quality and requires continuance; its loss drops the obligation.

In the previous lesson, we learned about enabling capacity (qudrat al-tamkin), which is a pure condition for capability without altering the obligation. Now we encounter a second type: easing capacity (qudrat al-muyassira). This capacity is a special grace from Allah that makes obligations easy and lenient. The key difference is that this capacity changes the very quality of the obligation, so its continuance is required for the obligation to persist. If the easing capacity is lost, the obligation falls because it was legislated with that easy quality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of capacity
Read the passage and note that the first capacity (qudrat al-tamkin) is for capability, the second (qudrat al-muyassira) is easing. Highlight the phrase 'زائدة على الأولى بدرجة'.
The first type was covered in the previous lesson.
2
Analyze the effect on the obligation
For the first type, the obligation does not change; it remains a pure condition. For the second, the obligation's quality changes to 'samhan, sahlan, layyinan' (generous, easy, gentle).
This change is crucial for the rule of continuance.
3
Derive the rule of continuance
Since the obligation's quality is tied to the easing capacity, the capacity must persist for the obligation to persist. When the capacity ceases, the obligation ceases because it was not legislated without that description.
This is the core legal principle.
4
Apply to the Zakat example
The passage states that if the nisab (minimum threshold) is destroyed after the year, Zakat drops. This is because Zakat is attached to an easing capacity (having surplus wealth). The loss of nisab removes the easing capacity, so the obligation falls.
Contrast with enabling capacity: if the obligation only required enabling capacity, later loss would not affect it.
5
Generalize the principle
From this, understand that in Hanafi usul, obligations that are based on easing capacity (e.g., Zakat, possibly other financial duties) are conditional on the continuance of that capacity. This is a mercy from Allah.
This principle helps determine when obligations persist or drop.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكامل من هذا القسم : فالقدرة الميسرة، وهذه زائدة على الأُولى بدرجة؛ كرامة من الله تعالى. وفَرْقُ ما بين الأمرين أن القدرة الأولى للتمكن من الفعل، فلم يتغير بها الواجب، فبقي شرطاً محضاً ، فلم يُشترط دوامها لبقاء الواجب. وهذه لما كانت قدرة ميسرة : غيّرت صفة الواجب، فجعلته سمحاً سهلاً ليّناً، فشرط بقاء هذه القدرة لبقاء الواجب، لا لمعنى أنها شرط، ولكن لمعنى تبدل صفة الواجب بها، فإذا انقطعت هذه القدرة : بطل ذلك الوصف، فيبطل الحق ؛ لأنه غير مشروع بدون ذلك الوصف.
- ولهذا قلنا : إن الزكاة تسقط بهلاك النصاب بعد الحول؛ لأن الشرع علّق الوجوب بقدرة ميسرة، ألا يُرى أن القدرة على الأداء تحصل بمال مطلق. ثم شرط النماء في المال؛ ليكون المؤدى جزءاً منه، فيكون في غاية
As for the perfect one of this section: it is the easing capacity, and this exceeds the first by one degree; as a grace from Allah. And the difference between the two matters is that the first capacity is for capability of the act, so the obligation does not change by it, so it remains a pure condition, so its continuance is not required for the persistence of the obligation. And this one, since it is an easing capacity, changed the quality of the obligation, making it generous, easy, gentle, so the continuance of this capacity is required for the persistence of the obligation, not because it is a condition, but because of the change in quality of the obligation by it. So when this capacity ceases, that description is nullified, so the right becomes nullified; because it is not legislated without that description. And for this we say: Zakat is dropped by the destruction of the nisab after the year; because the law attached the obligation to an easing capacity. Do you not see that the capacity to perform is obtained by absolute wealth. Then he required growth in the wealth, so that what is given is a part of it, so it is at the utmost...
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of capacity (qudrat al-muyassira), which is a special grace from Allah, beyond the first type (qudrat al-tamkin). First capacity simply enables the act without altering the obligation, so it is a pure condition; the obligation remains even if that capacity is later lost (as seen in the previous lesson). In contrast, the easing capacity changes the very quality of the obligation, making it easy and lenient. Therefore, the obligation depends on the continuance of this easing capacity; if it disappears, the obligation also drops because the obligation was legislated with this easy quality. The practical example is Zakat: Zakat is obligatory when one possesses the nisab (minimum threshold) for a year, but the obligation is based on an easing capacity (having disposable wealth). If the nisab is destroyed after the year but before payment, Zakat is dropped (according to this view). The condition of growth (nama') in wealth is also tied to this easing capacity, because Zakat is taken from the growth.

Key Terms
القدرة الميسرة (al-qudrat al-muyassira): A capacity that eases the obligation, changing its quality to ease, and requiring its continuance for the obligation to persist.
النصاب (al-nisāb): The minimum amount of wealth that makes Zakat obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Zakat is dropped because the easing capacity no longer exists.
C) Zakat must be paid from the remaining nisab.
D) Zakat is postponed until the nisab is regained.

Key Takeaway
Easing capacity (qudrat al-muyassira) changes the quality of an obligation and requires its continuance; its loss causes the obligation to fall, as illustrated by the dropping of Zakat upon destruction of the nisab.

Two Types of Ability (Qudra) and Their Effects on Obligations
Explaining two types of ability and their legal implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of ability (qudra) in Hanafi usul and apply the rule regarding their effect on the continuance of obligations.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, mere enabling ability does not need to persist; facilitated ability must continue, or the obligation lapses.

In this passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul, we explore a crucial distinction in Hanafi legal theory: the difference between ability that merely enables an act and ability that facilitates it. This distinction determines whether an obligation persists when the ability disappears. Understanding this is key to grasping Hanafi rulings on obligations like zakat and expiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of ability
Locate the author's description of the first type (mere enabling) and the second (facilitated ability). Note the Arabic terms: القدرة الأولى and القدرة الميسرة.
2
Understand the effect of the first type
The first type is a condition for performance but not for continuance. The obligation persists even if the ability ceases. Example: Hajj obligation remains even if wealth perishes.
See the text: 'إذا مَلَكَ الزاد والراحلة، فلم يحج حتى هلك المال: لم يبطل عنه الحج.'
3
Understand the effect of the second type
The second type is a facilitated ability that changes the nature of the obligation. Its continuance is required; if it ceases, the obligation lapses. Example: Zakat lapses with perishing of nisab.
See the text: 'الزكاة تسقط بهلاك النصاب بعد الحول.'
4
Analyze the rationale for the difference
The first type is a pure condition, so it does not alter the obligation. The second type makes the obligation easy, so without it, the obligation is no longer legislated.
The text says: 'هذه لما كانت قدرة ميسرة : غيّرت صفة الواجب، فجعلته سمحاً سهلاً ليّناً.'
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Consider the case of expiation for a broken oath: if a wealthy person becomes poor after breaking the oath, the obligation changes to fasting because the original obligation was tied to facilitated ability.
See the text: 'الموسر إذا حنث في اليمين، ثم أعسر وذهب ماله : أنه يكفر بالصوم.'

ولهذا قلنا : إن الزكاة تسقط بهلاك النصاب بعد الحول؛ لأن الشرع علّق الوجوب بقدرة ميسرة، ألا يُرى أن القدرة على الأداء تحصل بمال مطلق. ثم شرط النماء في المال؛ ليكون المؤدى جزءاً منه، فيكون في غاية التيسير، فلو قلنا ببقاء الواجب بدون النصاب : لانقلب غرامةً محضة، فيتبدل الواجب، فلذلك سقط بهلاك المال. ولا يلزم أن النصاب شرط لابتداء الوجوب، ولا يشترط لبقائه، فإن كل جزء من الباقي يبقى بقسطه ؛ لأن شرط النصاب لا يُغيّر صفةَ الواجب. ألا يُرى أن تيسير أداء الخمسة من المئتين وتيسير أداء الدرهم من الأربعين سواء، لا يختلف؛ لأنه ربع عشر بكل حال. لكن الغنى وصفٌ لا بدَّ منه ليصير الموصوف به أهلاً للإغناء، إذ الإغناء من غير الغني لا يتحقق، كالتمليك من غير المالك، والغنى بكثرة المال، وليس للكثرة حد تُعرف به، وأحوال الناس فيه شتى، فقدر الشرع بحد واحد، فصار ذلك شرطاً للوجوب لما كان أمراً زائداً على الأهلية الأصلية. وشَرْطُ الوجوب: لا يُشترط دوامه، إذ الوجوب في واجب واحد لا يتكرر، فأما قيام المال بصفة النماء فميسر للأداء ، فتغير به صفة الواجب، فشَرَطنا دوامه. وهذا بخلاف استهلاك النصاب، فإنه لا يُسقط الحق، وقد صار غُرْماً؛ لأن النصاب صار في حق الواجب حقاً لصاحب الحق، فيصير المستهلك متعدياً على صاحب الحق ، فعد قائماً في حق صاحب الحق، فصار الواجب على هذا التقدير غير متبدل. And the judgment: as for when performance is missed while having ability due to the addressee's negligence, then it remains under his responsibility, and the condition is treated as if still present legally due to his negligence. As for when performance is missed not due to his negligence, likewise; because this ability was a condition for the obligation of performance, as a favor from Allah Almighty, so it was not stipulated for the continuance of the obligation. Hence we said: it is not nullified by death regarding the rulings of the Hereafter. Hence we said: when one owns provisions and means of transport, and does not perform Hajj until the wealth perishes, Hajj is not canceled for him. Likewise, Zakat al-Fitr does not lapse with the perishing of wealth, for what we mentioned. As for the complete [type] of this category: it is the facilitated ability, and this exceeds the first by a degree, as honor from Allah Almighty. The difference between the two is that the first ability is for enabling the act, so the obligation did not change with it, so it remained a pure condition, thus its continuance for the continuance of the obligation was not required. As for this, when it is a facilitated ability, it changed the nature of the obligation, made it easy, gentle, lenient, so the continuance of this ability was required for the continuance of the obligation, not because it is a condition, but because the nature of the obligation changes with it. So when this ability ceases, that description is invalidated, so the right becomes invalid because it is not legislated without that description. Hence we said: Zakat lapses with the perishing of the nisab after the year, because the Lawgiver made the obligation dependent on a facilitated ability. Do you not see that the ability to perform is achieved with absolute wealth. Then growth (nama') in wealth was required so that the paid amount becomes part of it, making it extremely easy. If we said the obligation persists without the nisab, it would turn into a pure indemnity, changing the obligation, so it lapses with the perishing of wealth. And it does not follow that the nisab is a condition for the inception of the obligation and not required for its continuance, because each part of the remainder continues proportionally, because the condition of nisab does not change the nature of the obligation. Do you not see that the ease of paying five from two hundred and the ease of paying one dirham from forty is the same, it does not differ, because it is one-fourth of a tenth in any case. But wealth (ghina) is an attribute that must be present for the described to become suitable for giving, since giving from one without wealth is not realized, like giving ownership from one who does not own. Wealth is by abundance of property, and there is no known limit for abundance, and people's states therein vary. So the Lawgiver determined it with one limit, so that became a condition for obligation since it was an additional matter beyond the original eligibility. And the condition of obligation: its continuance is not required, because obligation in a single obligatory act is not repeated. As for the wealth being in a state of growth, it facilitates performance, so it changes the nature of the obligation, so we required its continuance. And this differs from consuming the nisab, for it does not nullify the right, and it becomes a liability, because the nisab becomes a right of the claimant, so the consumer becomes transgressor against the claimant, so it is considered as existing in the claimant's right, and the obligation on this estimation is unchanged. And for this we said: if a wealthy person breaks his oath, then becomes poor and his wealth disappears, he expiates by fasting, because the obligation is dependent on facilitated ability.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul is a legal analysis of the concept of ability (qudra) and its role in the persistence of obligations. The author distinguishes two types of ability. The first is a mere condition for the obligation to perform (shart li-wujub al-ada'). If a person had this ability but missed the performance through negligence, the obligation remains and is treated as if the ability still exists due to his fault. If he missed it without negligence, the obligation also remains because this ability was a favor from Allah, not required for the duty's continuance. Examples: Hajj obligation persists even if wealth perishes after one becomes able; Sadaqat al-Fitr does not lapse if the wealth perishes after the obligation is due. The second type is 'facilitated ability' (qudra muyassara), which exceeds the first in honor. This ability actually changes the nature of the obligation, making it easy and flexible. Therefore, its continuance is required for the obligation to persist; if it ceases, the obligation lapses because it is not legislated without that facilitative quality. Example: Zakat lapses if the nisab perishes after the year because the Lawgiver based the obligation on this facilitated ability. The text further argues that nisab is a condition for the inception of obligation but not for its continuance, illustrated by the fact that if part of the nisab remains, zakat is due proportionally. However, if one consumes the nisab intentionally, it does not nullify the obligation because the nisab becomes a liability (ghurm) in the claimant's right. Finally, the expiation for a broken oath: if a wealthy person becomes poor after breaking the oath, the obligation changes to fasting because the original obligation was linked to facilitated ability.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra): Ability to perform an action, of two types: mere enabling and facilitated.
القدرة الميسرة (al-qudra al-muyassara): A superior ability that makes the obligation easy and changes its nature; its continuance is required for the obligation's persistence.
شرط الوجوب (shart al-wujub): A condition for the obligation to become due; its continuance is not required once the obligation is established.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because nisab is a condition for the inception of obligation, not for its continuance.
B) Because the obligation becomes too difficult without wealth.
C) Because zakat is tied to facilitated ability; without nisab, the obligation would lose its easy nature.
D) Because the wealth is no longer productive.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between mere enabling ability and facilitated ability in Hanafi usul determines whether an obligation persists or lapses when the ability ceases.
